==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This thesis examines the possibilities for renovating Greenlandic apartment blocks constructed in the
1960s and 1970s. The main purpose has been to investigate strategies that optimize the thermally
outdated envelope.
Through an extensive case study of existing apartment blocks in Sisimiut, it is found that the existing
building mass poses as a great potential for undergoing a renovation. The strict system of the buildings
simplifies the process of creating a new system for managing a renovation. Additionally, a great potential
is seen in reusing the existing load bearing structure and the existing infrastructures. Furthermore, the
well designed apartments and the fact that the load bearing concrete structure has been protected by
the non bearing envelope emphasizes a renovation project. However the facade is outdated and many
thermal bridges in the envelope are influencing the energy performance negatively. Thus, this thesis
investigates renovation strategies that improve the energy performance of the apartment blocks, where
special attention is given to the thermal bridges. The most critical thermal bridges are related to the
cantilevers supporting the balconies, gable wings and joints between the external slabs and load bearing
walls.
It is found that the thermal bridges have a significant impact on the energy performance. This is
more evident if an existing facade is replaced without handling the thermal bridges. For well insulated
wall systems (400 mm insulation thickness) the thermal bridges in the cantilevers account for more than
25 % of the heat loss through a facade element, whereas this is as high as 50 % for the gable wings.
Additionally, an analysis of the overall energy consumption of the apartment blocks is included in the
thesis. The analysis shows that the existing buildings are exceeding the current requirement, where
both the outdated thermal performance and the poor air tightness contribute to that. In addition
to implementing a new improved facade system, the analysis shows that a new ventilation system
with heat recovery is essential to reduce the energy consumption. Furthermore, the thermal bridges
need to be handled to increase the energy performance. Additionally, an analysis of possible moisture
issues resulting from low indoor surface temperatures near thermal bridges emphasizes that the thermal
bridges are important to handle during renovation. This is especially evident in Greenland with low
outdoor temperatures.
Based on the aforementioned, two renovation proposals are presented, where the thermal bridges in
the facade have been eliminated. The energy consumption of both proposals is reduced to more than
one fourth of the existing buildings. Additionally, through investigations on the functional design, it is
shown that various initiatives regarding layout of floor plans, accessibility and building form, can lead
==PAGE:6==
ii
Abstract
to development of not only better insulated buildings, but also better homes. Furthermore, a principle
of buildability is presented in the thesis, where prefabricated facade elements similar to the existing
are mounted to the load bearing structures. By utilizing the existing facade system, the renovation
strategy is not limited to the analyzed apartment blocks in Sisimiut, but can be applied with minor
modifications in many settings in Greenland.
==PAGE:7==
Preface
This thesis was prepared at the department of Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark
in fulfillment of the requirements for acquiring a Master of Science degree in Architectural Engineering.
The thesis has been produced in close collaboration between the two authors and is credited 35 ECTS
credit for each student.
The product of the thesis includes the main report and appendix. The appendix consists of detailed
drawings, calculations and additional assessments which are considered too extensive to be included in
the main report. Additionally, a digital appendix has been produced containing relevant models for the
thesis. Unless other is stated, all illustrations, renderings and photos are produced by the authors of
the thesis.
The layout of the thesis is based on Laursen’s thesis template, and is intended to be read in a two
page view. The thesis is best read in color. The front page is an illustration based on the layout of
typical Greenlandic apartment blocks.
Primary supervisor: Tove Lading, Technical University of Denmark
Secondary supervisor: Toke Rammer Nielsen, Technical University of Denmark
Andreas Plaugborg Pedersen
s133608
Charlotte Kloster Haugvaldstad
s121689

Acknowledgements
There are several people who have contributed to the realization of this thesis. We wish to give our
thanks to our primary supervisor, Tove Lading. Your guidance and sharing of your extensive knowledge
on renovation and building in Greenland has been valuable for our project. Additionally, we wish to
thank Toke Rammer Nielsen, our co-supervisor. We greatly appreciate the time you have taken for
giving guidance regarding our Energy analysis.
We wish to thank Artek for allowing us to enroll in the preliminary courses of the Arctic Semester,
preparing us for writing a thesis on renovation and building technology in the Greenlandic context.
Additionally, we wish to thank people who we have met on our path in Greenland. They have
provided us with both knowledge and materials regarding the buildings. These include:
Dennis Holm (INI, Sismiut)
Peter Barfoed (Tegnestuen Nuuk)
Jørn Hansen (Rambøll, Nuuk)
Lastly, we would like to thank Martin Kotol and Tom Sven Siegemund for their valuable input and
discussions on Greenlandic building technology.

CHAPTER 1
Introduction and formalities
1.1
Motivation
In the 1960’s and 70’s the Danish government started constructing multistory buildings for handling the
increasing housing demand due to centralization in the cities of Greenland. The buildings resembled
the apartment buildings constructed in Denmark and the rest of Europe during the time. Harsh use of
the buildings and extreme weather conditions, in combination with lack of maintenance have taken its
toll on the buildings and have in some cases resulted in uninhabitable buildings. The mass-produced
buildings are now in a state where some sort of handling is due. Today, the decaying buildings are
present in the townscape in the majority of cities along the western coast of Greenland, and run-down
areas with a collection of dull apartment blocks are not an uncommon sight.
In Greenland there is an ongoing debate on whether the existing building mass should be demolished
or renovated. Due to the housing demand in Greenland, the buildings can not be demolished without
ensuring development of new apartments for substituting the removal. But new development of build-
ings in Greenland is a costly affair. The majority of the building materials must be imported, and long
travel distances and lack of infrastructure between cities are also challenges for facilitating building de-
velopment in Greenland. A renovation of the existing building mass, opens for the possibility of reusing
elements from previous projects and integrating it within a new building design, and therefore removing
or reducing some areas of expense. Additionally, land development for roads and securing water and
sewer supply is troublesome in Greenland due to the natural geological and climate conditions. A ren-
ovation of the buildings gives the possibility of utilizing the existing infrastructure already supporting
the buildings. A key motivation for investigating renovation possibilities, is the potential of economical
advantages of reusing existing building mass. Additionally, reuse of existing building elements has a
great potential of reducing the environmental impacts associated with the construction of a building.
This is especially evident in a remote place like Greenland where the environmental aspect associated
with transport of building materials could influence the environmental impact significantly.
Renovation of building in Greenland is however very uncommon and there is a lack of experience
within the field.
Thus, the few renovation projects have been found too expensive, resulting in a
reluctance to renovate. However, experiences of renovating buildings in Greenland is essential to lowering
the cost. The buildings constitute a large portion of the total building mass in Greenland. The buildings
are all based on the same elemental system and principles, only varying in height and length. Taking
advantage of the similarity creates the opportunity to find a renovation solution that can potentially
be unfolded onto a large scale.
==PAGE:22==
2
1 Introduction and formalities
Due to these arguments, it is found reasonable to conduct an evaluation of the possibilities for
transforming the decaying building mass into energy efficient, well designed apartments fit for the
modern Greenlandic context.
1.2
Project formalities
Based on the aforementioned motivation, the title and problem statement of this thesis are as follows:
Title:
Renovation of Greenlandic apartment blocks constructed in the
1960s and 1970s, with a focus on improving the thermal envelope
Problem statement:
This thesis examines renovation principles of Greenlandic apart-
ment blocks mass produced in the 1960s and 1970s, by focusing on a
specific renovation case of unmaintained apartment blocks in Sisim-
iut. These buildings are characterized by a load bearing concrete
inner structure and a non bearing facade of light facade elements.
The aim is to analyze the current condition of the apartment blocks,
where the constraints and possibilities in the existing building mass
are assessed. The main focus is to investigate the thermal envelope
of the apartment blocks and to identify possible thermal bridges.
Through a study of the energy performance of the existing buildings
and a set of renovation proposals, this thesis aims to propose a
concept for a final renovation proposal. The handling of existing
thermal bridges is a key aspect in this process.
The objective of the renovation proposal is to introduce a system
that utilizes the extent of similarity in the existing building mass.
Thus, the renovation strategy is not limited to the apartment blocks
in Sisimiut, but can be applied in many settings in Greenland. The
aim is that these solutions can serve as an inspiration for future
renovations of similar apartment blocks in Greenland.
The project is a Master Thesis and is part of the completion of the degree of Master in Architectural
Engineering from the Technically University of Denmark. The work in the thesis is equal to 35 ECTS
credits for each student.
The project was a part of a 4 month stay in Sisimiut, Greenland in the
spring of 2018, where both authors were enrolled in the Arctic Semester Programme from the Technical
University of Denmark. The thesis was carried out in Copenhagen, Denmark during the fall and winter
2018/2019.
1.3
Project scope
A renovation project requires many in depth analyses in several fields of specialization within the
building design. To conduct a fully detailed investigation of all elements in a renovation proposal is
considered too extensive. Therefore, it has been important to limit the scope of this thesis, allowing
an appropriate degree of detail in some chosen elements of the analysis. In the following the scope of
==PAGE:23==
1.3 Project scope
3
the project is presented, where the main focus of the thesis is explained as well as important elements
within the renovation process that have been excluded are accounted for.
Optimization of the energy performance is one of the important aspects within the renovation process
and in an arctic climate the thermal envelope accounts for a major part of the energy consumption.
Thus, the main focus of this thesis is to investigate the thermal performance of the existing building
and possible renovation solutions, where particularly the building envelope is examined. This includes
detailed analyses of the thermal bridges in the building, as they account for a significant part of the
heat loss in the existing construction. In addition to the thermal envelope, the ventilation system has a
great impact on the energy performance of a building and thus considerations on the ventilation design
are included in the project. However, a detailed dimensioning of the ventilation system has not been
included.
The main purpose of the analysis is to get an overview of which initiatives are effective when
optimizing the thermal performance of the building. Based on the results a final concept for a renovation
proposal is presented, where design choices regarding functionality are included. However, since the
aim of this thesis is to investigate renovation proposals on a conceptual level, designing of all details
has not been made.
One of the key elements in the renovation process is assessing the existing building mass. Multiple
inspections of the analyzed apartment blocks have been made, and as the focus in this project is
related to energy performance, investigations related to this have been prioritized.
Therefore, the
structural capacities of the existing blocks have not been examined. This is however a key element
when determining if a renovation project is possible. In the thesis the load bearing capacities have been
assumed to be sufficient for reuse without too much repair. This assumption is a key prerequisite of the
following work, but has been found reasonable, based on conversations with competent people familiar
with the apartment blocks in Sisimiut.
This project does not include an analysis of the economic aspect of the project expenses. It has been
decided to omit this due to the high uncertainties connected to evaluating the cost of building, building
materials, transportation and other services regarding renovation in Greenland. Furthermore the time
frame of this project, has added to not including an economic analysis. Even though it is considered
that there are environmental benefits when renovating compared to demolition and developing new
buildings, an assessment of this has not been carried out in this project.
Lastly, one of the objectives in this thesis has been to create a renovation concept that can be applied
to apartment blocks in other parts of Greenland. This is possible as the existing apartments blocks in
Greenland are, if not identical, very similar and constructed within the same period. However, as minor
distinctions between the apartment blocks both in layout and condition might occur, the analysis in
this thesis is limited to the analyzed case. Furthermore, because of the size of Greenland, the climatic
conditions within the country varies greatly, meaning that some of the presented results are only valid
for the analyzed location.
==PAGE:24==
4
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 2
Methodology and process
2.1
Process and structure of the thesis
The main composition of this thesis is divided into four chapters: Pre study, Case study, Energy study
and Concept and design development. This is visualized in the diagram seen in Figure 2.1. The four
boxes represent the four main chapters in the project. Since the thesis includes different aspects within
the renovation process, the applied methodology for each analysis will be described in the respective
chapters. In the following the general methodology and the structure of the thesis is presented.
Pre study
Case study
Energy study
Concept and design
development
Renovation
proposal
Figure 2.1: Representation of project composition
Pre study
This chapter serves as an introduction to the Greenlandic apart-
ment blocks and the Greenlandic setting, prior to the analysis of
the apartment blocks in Sisimiut. This includes a description of the
apartment blocks’ origin, and an introduction to the Greenlandic
climate.
Case study
In this chapter the apartment blocks in Sisimiut are introduced as
the scope of reference.
The structure and layout of these build-
ings will be investigated as well as considerations on the condition
and presence of hazardous materials will be made. Furthermore,
thermography analyses of the buildings will be presented.
Energy study
In this chapter the existing apartment blocks as well as renovation
proposals, are analyzed regarding to the energy performance. The
chapter is divided into three parts, where the first part includes an
detailed analysis of the heat flow in the thermal bridges. Secondly
the overall energy consumption of the scenarios are simulated, and
lastly the thermal bridges are assessed in relation to moisture and
low surface temperatures.
==PAGE:26==
6
2 Methodology and process
Concept and de-
sign development
In this chapter the renovation proposals will be investigated regard-
ing the functional design. This includes considerations on acces-
sibility, layout of the apartments as well as implementation of a
ventilation system and a concept for buildability. The outcome of
this is a final renovation proposal.
The composition of this report is presented in a linear manner where the chapters are independent
and do not overlap.
This does not represent the work flow during the project.
The reality of the
thesis work has been conducted in a non-linear iterative process. An important part of this process has
been how investigations on energy performance and functionality have been influencing one another,
contributing to a continuous evolution in the design development. Through multiple iterative design
and simulation processes, the design development narrows towards a final renovation proposal based on
all assessments and considerations. The linear representation in the report ensures a more structured
composition for the sake of readability.
Figure 2.2: Representation of non-linear iterative process
The MSc program in Architectural Engineering focuses on linking acquired engineering skills within
the holistic design process.
Handling of different design parameters and understanding how design
choices influence different aspects of the building is a key element in building design. Through cross-
disciplinary projects the education equips the students with the capability of integrating their knowledge
of engineering in the design processes for striving towards well-integrated and optimal solutions. In this
thesis, the specialization in energy design has been integrated in the design process for handling the
renovation of apartment blocks in Greenland.

was used to simulate the energy consumption of the existing buildings as well as the renovation proposals.

CHAPTER 3
Pre study
In this chapter general observations regarding the Greenlandic apartment blocks are presented. This
includes a description of the origin and development of the apartment blocks and a brief explanation of
the extent of similarity in the existing building mass. Furthermore, key parameters in relation to the
Greenlandic climate will be presented and compared to the Danish climate.
3.1
Considerations on the development, maintenance and future
prospect for concrete apartment buildings in Greenland
In the late 1950s the first apartment block were constructed in Greenland, and in the following period
a mass production of similar buildings were carried out in many of the biggest Greenlandic cities [44].
Throughout the years, the stakeholders accountable for the development and maintenance of the con-
crete apartment buildings in Greenland has shifted. The original development of the buildings can be
traced back to the Greenland’s Technical Organization (GTO), established by the Danish Government.
Today, the Government of Greenland owns many of the buildings, where the Greenlandic housing as-
sociation (INI) holds the responsibility for administrative handling and maintenance of the buildings.
Several political initiatives and the complex relationship between Denmark and Greenland have influ-
enced the development and maintenance of the apartment blocks. In the following, the vision behind
the development of the apartment blocks and the initiatives leading to the current state will be briefly
explained.
Figure 3.1: The newly constructed Block P in Nuuk[33]
==PAGE:30==
10
3 Pre study
3.1.1
GTO’s establishment and development of multistory housing
In 1950 Greenland’s Technical Organization (GTO) was established [44]. The organization was responsi-
ble for all technical activity and handling of all construction work in Greenland. After its establishment,
GTO focused on construction of houses, schools, hospitals, infrastructure etc. However, in the late 50’s
it was eventually becoming more and more difficult to find accessible and suitable plots for single family
dwellings.
In the 1960’s the population in the towns grew rapidly. This was a result of the ambition from
Danish politicians where Greenland was to be modernized in a efficient manner. This resulted in a vision
to centralize the Greenlandic population from the settlements to the Greenlandic cities. In addition
health services combating tuberculosis and a drop in the infant-mortality rate had a positive effect on
the population growth [41]. GTO had difficulty covering the increasing demand for houses in the cities
solely through building single-family houses, while solving the hygienic problems in a financially sound
way (high cost for establishing sewer and water supply). This resulted in that buildings had to be
concentrated additionally, and it was decided to build multistory houses in up to five stories.
In addition to this, building in Greenland proved to be much more cost demanding than what the
Danes where used to from home. The natural conditions in Greenland proved that developing land for
housing, laying foundation, securing water and sewage supply and conveying roads was very expensive
compared to building in Denmark.
Besides the natural obstacles, lack of local building materials
resulted in that all materials, except gravel, rock and sand, needed to be imported from Denmark.
A combination of large distances between developments and relatively few people each place led to
an ineffective expenditure. A concentration and centralization of dwellings was therefore an efficient
method for reducing the land development expenses.
The first multistory building in Greenland was built in the late 50’s and was built for the government
officials from Denmark, who were used to this type of residential form from Denmark [44]. To provide
a rapid housing solution for the increasing demand, multistory apartment buildings was an efficient
and quick procedure. However, many thoughts and considerations of the inhabitants were lost in the
process.
The buildings lacked consideration for the Greenlandic way of life, as they were replicas
of the multistory flats built in Denmark and the rest of Europe during that time. In the following
period, a mass construction of these concrete apartment blocks were carried out in many of the biggest
Greenlandic cities, where the construction peaked in the 1960s and 1970s. In line with the ongoing
criticism on the building method in Denmark and in the rest of Europe the construction of the concrete
apartment blocks was phased out in the late 1970s and 1980s.
In the construction period, the commission believed that the building activity would be a short-
term process and it was thought to be insufficient to involve the local population in the construction
process [44]. The general attitude was that the Greenlanders should pursue hunting and fishing, while
construction workers were brought in from Denmark. In a report produced by GTO in 1984 it is stated
that the Greenlanders were not capable of building houses to a satisfactory quality and lacked the
actual craftsman like tradition on house building [29]. In retrospect, it is seen that these predictions
==PAGE:31==
3.2 Presence of apartment blocks in Greenland
11
and assumptions towards the building activity proved wrong and contradicting. Wrong in the sense that
the building activity in Greenland continued in an ascending degree and contradicting in assuming that
there would not be need for local construction workers after finishing the constructions. This indicates
that the maintenance of the buildings was not considered. If all construction workers were imported
from Denmark, Greenland had no local craftsmen who had taken a part of the construction and thus
no knowledge on maintenance of the buildings.
3.1.2
The current situation and future prospects
Harsh use of the buildings and weather conditions has taken its toll on the condition of the apartment
buildings. In combination with lack of continuous maintenance the state of the buildings today has in
some cases resulted in uninhabitable buildings in decay.
Today, INI administrates the money granted from the Greenland Self Government for the purpose
of renovation. The funds are insufficient to keep the condition of the building blocks in Sisimiut on an
acceptable level [38]. Only two of the 13 building blocks have undergone a total renovation with new
facade, with improved insulation etc. Another five of the blocks have had new kitchens implemented,
and some of the apartments have been modified from four room apartments to three room apartments
where a scullery is added. The plan for the remaining six blocks in Sisimiut is still uncertain. This
picture is the same for many building blocks in the Greenlandic cities, that shows lack of maintenance
and a need for renovation.

3.3
Climate conditions in Greenland
With an area of 2,166,086 km2 Greenland earns the status of being the largest island in the world [28],
where 81% of the island is covered in ice. The island is located in the northern polar region and has
arctic climate, characterized by cold winters and mild summers where the average temperatures does
not exceed 10◦C even in the warmest summer months. Due to its size there are great weather variations
from region to region. Figure 3.12 shows the variation in dimensioning exterior temperatures through
Greenland. All towns and settlements are located along the coastline, which stretches 44,087 km. Most
habitation is located on the southern west coast. Sisimiut is located on the west coast, 75 km north of
the polar circle.
Figure 3.12: Dimensioning exterior temperatures [35]
==PAGE:34==
14
3 Pre study
The mean monthly values for temperature, wind speed, relative humidity and solar radiation is
shown on Figure 3.13 - 3.16. The values corresponds to weather data files representing Sisimiut and
Denmark ( More information on the origin of the climate data is described in Section 5.3.2 on review
of weather files used in energy analysis).
Figure 3.13 represents the mean air temperature through the year for Sisimiut and Denmark. March
is the coldest month of the year in Sisimiut with an average temperature of -17.1◦C, and the warmest
mean temperature is in July, with 8.5◦C. The wind speed for the two climate zones is represented
in Figure 3.14. It is seen that for all months except October, Sisimiut has greater wind speeds than
in danish climate. Especially in the winter months, there is a substantial difference between the two
locations.
Figure 3.15 shows the mean level of relative humidity for Sisimiut and Greenland.
The
climate in Greenland is characterized as very dry air with low moisture content.
In Section 5.4, a
thorough description of the moisture conditions in Sisimiut can be read. Figure 3.16 shows the solar
radiation for Denmark and Greenland. The graph shows both the direct solar radiation, the diffuse solar
radiation and the two combined in total solar radiation. Even though Sisimiut has a more northern
location, the total solar radiation is somewhat similar for the two locations. This might be the result of
a slightly higher direct solar radiation, which much likely is due to low moisture content in the arctic air.
Even though Sisimiut has less hours of sunshine during the winter period, the amount of direct solar
radiation contributes to a relatively high amount of total solar radiation. The diffuse solar radiation is
however slightly higher in Denmark. Although the sun is very low during winter in Greenland, the sun
is present most of the time during the summer periods.

3.4
Experiences from field study
As a preparation for writing this thesis, a four month period (February - May) was spent living in
Sisimiut and travelling in Greenland. The stay enabled a registration and inspection of the apartment
blocks. Furthermore it also allowed an establishment of contact with the different stakeholders involved
in the future of the apartment blocks which provided information and drawings relevant for the project.
Additionally, the first hand experience of living in Sisimiut gave a knowledge on how the climate effects
the daily life and what are the necessities when creating climate appropriate dwellings. Trips along the
==PAGE:36==

3 Pre study
west coast revealed to which extent the buildings are present in Greenland. Nuuk, Aasiaat, Ilulissat,
Maniitsoq and Sisimiut all had multistory buildings as a part of their townscape.

CHAPTER 4
Case study: Concrete
apartment buildings
To narrow the investigations on renovation of Greenlandic apartment blocks, the apartment blocks of
Paamaap Kuua in Sisimiut (also referred as ”the white blocks”) has been chosen as a scope of reference
in this thesis. The apartment blocks of Paamaap Kuua is located in the center of the town of Sisimiut.
The area is one of two areas in Sisimiut where a collection of apartment blocks from the aforementioned
period has been constructed. The other apartment blocks of Aqqartarfik have already gone through a
minor renovation.
The apartment blocks of Paamaap Kuua are all built after the same principle but varying in length
and height. A site plan of the apartment blocks is shown on Figure 4.1. As seen Block 11 and Block
12 has already been torn down, and a further demolition of Block 10 is being planned. The plan was
to demolish the rest of the apartment blocks as well, but that has recently changed. Currently (spring
2018) the apartment blocks (except Block 10) are a part of a renovation plan. The actually outcome of
this is however very uncertain, and the fate of Paamaap Kuua is likely to change again in the future.
As it stands today, Paamaap Kuua consists of seven blocks containing a total of 125 apartments
spread out on 7056 m2 [38].

In the following, the apartments blocks of Paamaap Kuua will be investigated in detail. The struc-
tural system and layout of the apartment blocks will be assessed as well as the current condition of
the buildings will be investigated. Furthermore potential hazardous risks regarding the old building
materials will be investigated. Lastly an thermography analysis of the buildings will be presented.
The work in this section sets the frame for the rest of the thesis and gives and overview of the current
defects and potentials. Furthermore, prerequisites and assumptions made will be explained, which is
relevant for the final renovation proposals.
4.1
Structure and layout
In this section the general structure of the apartment blocks in Sisimiut is described. Additionally
an overview of the spatial layout will be presented. In the end the existing building elements will be
assessed and details of their construction will be presented.
The work in this section is based on an extensive investigation of the available original drawings
made when the buildings were constructed as well as available drawings from the renovation of identical
apartment blocks in Sisimiut.
Furthermore, visual inspections has added to the relatively limited
amount of information from the drawings, as well as digital drawings from a renovation project of a
similar apartment block in Nuuk (Block T).
4.1.1
Structural system
The apartment blocks in Paamaap Kuua are all constructed after the same principles and are almost
identical. The buildings consist of four storeys except from Block 9, that only has three storeys. The
structural principle is very stringent, and as seen on the elevation of the teared down Block 12, Figure
4.4 the same structural system is repeated throughout the construction.
==PAGE:40==
20
4 Case study: Concrete apartment buildings
All apartment blocks have two facades with windows and doors, while the gable of the blocks are
solid with no openings. Furthermore balconies run along every apartment block at each level on both
facades (one common balcony for accessing the apartments and one private).
A vertical section of the demolished block 12 and an elevation of the gable is seen on Figure 4.5. The
private balconies that are connected to every apartment is seen on the left side of the vertical section,
while the balconies on the right serve as access balconies to the apartments. By one of the two internal
staircases (see Figure 4.6) you are lead up to the access balconies from where you have access to the
apartments on the upper floors.
Figure 4.4: Elevation of the eastern facade of Block 12 (GTO drawing)
Figure 4.5: Vertical section of Block 12 (left) and elevation of north/south gable (right) (GTO drawing)
==PAGE:41==
4.1 Structure and layout
21
The slabs on every floor as well as the balcony cantilevers and the balconies themselves are made of
reinforced concrete, while the roof is a triangular timber construction. The facade is non bearing while
the internal walls perpendicular to the facade are taking all the loads. Theses walls forms the load
bearing system of the buildings, together with the gables and a few other inner walls that are parallel
to the facade which creates stability (two are located near the staircases). The load bearing walls are
made of reinforced concrete and are shown on a floor plan of Block 12, Figure 4.6. Looking closely at
the gables, it is seen that they extend further out than where the facade is positioned. This part is
called a gable wing and is potentially a severe thermal bridge. Together with the cantilevers they are
supporting the balconies.
The structural system is identical for all the apartment blocks, however the length of the buildings
vary slightly. On Figure 4.7 the load bearing structure of Block 11 is exposed during the period when
being demolished.
Figure 4.6: Load bearing walls shown on a floor plan from Block 12
Figure 4.7: The load bearing structure exposed on Block 11 while being torn down (Photo taken by Egil
Borchersen)
==PAGE:42==
22
4 Case study: Concrete apartment buildings
4.1.2
Foundation
Figure 4.8 shows the foundation principle of the apartment blocks. The foundation is founded on piles
that are secured to the underlying bedrock. On top of the piles, reinforced concrete beams have been
cast in both direction. As seen these beams are not in direct contact with the ground, but are resting on
the piles. This creates a crawl space underneath the building. Along the edges of the building footprint,
concrete has been cast, meaning that the crawl space is not exposed to the outdoor. The cast concrete
around the building edges is however thermally insulated in the joints between the ground floor slab.
This will be investigated in detail in Section 4.4, Themography analysis. Beside that, the foundation is
poorly insulated which will be shown on a detailed drawing in Section 4.1.5.
In addition to the foundation, the structural system of the block is shown on Figure 4.8. The load
bearing walls are throughout every apartment block located with a distance of either 360 cm or 270 cm
between them. The distance between the floors is 280 cm.
Figure 4.8: Foundation of Block 12 (GTO drawing)
4.1.3
Facade elements
As mentioned, the facade elements of the apartment blocks do not contribute to the load bearing
system. These non bearing outer walls consist of a light wooden construction with 125mm insulation
(see Section 4.1.5 for detailed drawing).
Furthermore, all windows and doors are located in these
elements. As mention the blocks are constructed in such a way that the distance between the load
bearing walls is either 360 cm or 270 cm. This creates a system where in principle only two different
kinds of compositions of facade elements has to be created. These two compositions are seen on Figure
4.9. One consists of two window elements and a more narrow door element, and one consists of two
window elements. The two combinations consist of independent framework structure elements varying
in width (90 or 120 cm). Windows are located in the widest facade element whereas doors are placed
in the other. As seen on the previously floor plan of Block 12, Figure 4.6, the extra space between the
==PAGE:43==
4.1 Structure and layout
23
load bearing walls (from 360 cm to 270 cm) has been added whenever there is a need for an entrance
to an apartment. In the top corners the cantilevers that are carrying the balconies is seen penetrating
the envelope. To the right on Figure 4.9 a more detailed drawing of one of the facade elements with a
window is shown, where the wooden framework in the element is clearly shown. Furthermore, if looking
closely, it is seen that the thickness of the insulation layer at the slabs is decreased.
Figure 4.9: Facade elements of the apartment blocks (drawings made by SANATI based on GTO drawing)
4.1.4
Gable
The gable consists of one massive concrete wall, which is insulated on the outside.
The insulation
layer consists of two 50 mm insulation layers in a wooden framework as seen on Figure 4.10. Detailed
drawings of the gable and the gable wing can be seen in Section 4.1.5.
Figure 4.10: The gable of Block 11 while being teared down, where the insulation and wooden framework in
the gable is seen (Photo taken by Egil Borchersen)

4.1.5
Details of building elements
On the following page, detailed drawings of different building parts is shown.
A) Drawing A shows a vertical section of a part of the non bearing facade element, which consists of
insulation in a wooden framework.
B) On drawing B a part of the stairwell wall is shown, and as seen the insulation thickness is quite
limited.
C) A detail of the joint between the facade and the interior concrete slab is seen on drawing C.
In the joint the insulation thickness is limited. The drawing also shows where the cantilever is
joining the concrete load bearing inner walls (these are not a part of the shown section and is
thus marked with dashed lines). The cantilevers penetrates as shown the envelope, which causes
a severe thermal bridge. The thickness of the cantilever as well as the concrete inner walls is 15
cm. The construction of the cantilevers will be elaborated in Chapter 5 - Energy study.
D) The construction of the gable is shown on drawing D. It consists of two layers on insulation in a
wooden framework.
E) A detail of the short gable wing is seen on drawing E. The gable wing is as seen a potential severe
thermal bridge that will be analyzed in Chapter 5.
F) The roof is angled such that the snow can slide off. In addition to this, the attic is cold ensuring
that the snow on the outside of the roof is not heated from the interior, causing the snow to
melt (this will much likely happen if the roof is not insulated sufficiently). The melted snow can
potentially freeze again leading to frost formations, that can accumulate and cause damage to the
roof and otherwise pose as a danger for the residents. Instead the slab of the roof is insulated as
shown on drawing F.
G) On drawing G a section of the cast concrete ground floor slab is seen. The ground floor slab
and the load bearing inner walls are resting on the aforementioned foundation beams that again
are resting on the foundation piles. The ground floor slab and the foundation beams are poorly
insulated as seen which is leading to a big heat loss.

4.1.7
Ventilation system
The ventilation system in the existing apartment blocks consists of exhaustion in bathrooms and
kitchens, whereas the fresh air supply is provided through fresh air inlets in the facade, as well as
infiltration through the envelope that is not airtight. The ducts are located in shafts where also other
pipes are found such as pipes for water supply. The shafts in which the ventilation ducts are located are
shown on Figure 4.15. For every apartment the ducts are located just by the bathrooms and kitchens.
The shafts containing the ducts for the exhaustion in the kitchens are however located in the entrances
next to the kitchens.
Figure 4.15: Principle of ventilation system, showing shafts in Block 12, where ventilation ducts are located
(illustration based on GTO drawing)
==PAGE:49==
4.2 State and condition
29
4.2
State and condition
An inspection of the Paamaap Kuua apartment buildings in Sisimiut was conducted for validating the
state and condition of the buildings.
Both an external inspection of the building envelope and an
internal inspection of the apartments was performed. All six buildings where evaluated externally. For
the internal inspection, Greenland’s public housing association, INI, provided access to two apartments,
a four room apartment on the ground floor and a single room apartment on the first floor.
Both
apartments where located in Block 8.
Figure 4.16: Facade of Block 7
To evaluate the condition of the building, a grading system was established for evaluating each
building element. The grading system is based on four steps. The value of the scoring is described
below.
1. Good The building part is in good shape, with minor insignificant flaws.
2. Acceptable The building part is in acceptable shape, and can be brought to good condition with
some maintenance.
3. Middle The building part is in poor condition. There are several issues which must be repaired in
the near future to avoid any failures, violation of safety or damage to other building elements.
4. Insufficient The building element is in a unacceptable condition, and repairs must be executed
promptly to avoid any failures, violations of safety or damages to other building part.
The evaluation and scoring of the different building elements can be found in the full report produced
in relation with the inspection enclosed in Appendix D. The following section summarizes the main
conclusions drawn from the inspection.
==PAGE:50==
30
4 Case study: Concrete apartment buildings
4.2.1
Main conclusions from the inspection
External inspection
The first impression when visiting the area is a meeting with unmaintained buildings in need of a coat of
paint. Furthermore, presence of clutter gathering around the buildings and hanging from the balconies,
adds to a run-down expression of the area. A closer inspection of the exterior reveals areas on the
gable wing where the steel reinforcement on the concrete was exposed. Most likely due frost erosion by
water penetrating cracks in the concrete and expanding when freezing. Besides this, the visible concrete
seemed to be in good condition. There was no traces of subsidence or cracks seen in the foundation.
The facade and gable had a faded and aging expression with several traces of vandalism and graffiti.
Some of the eternit plates where damaged or had been replaced, but the majority was intact. The
balcony parapets had several signs of structural failure. Several temporary repairs are conducted, and
some parapets where completely replaced with a temporary solution. However, the cantilevers where
all intact but also in need of a layer of paint.
The windows are the original windows but with some modification. The original window is a single
pane window, however at some point another layer of glass has been fixed to the original window to
improve the thermal properties. There are several instances where the new layer had detached itself
from the frame, thus the benefits are gone. The general condition of the windows, is that they are old
windows that do not fulfill today’s standard.
Another observation from the inspection was the vandalism of the stairwell. The windows in the
stairwells in several of the apartment blocks where broken or completely missing, resulting in outdoor
temperatures inside the stairwell. Additionally there was seen several cases with graffiti on the interior
of the stairwell.

Internal inspection
One of the main observations from the internal inspection was that the condition of the two inspected
apartments where very different. The single room apartment was in such a critical state that it was not
suitable for rental in the current situation. The apartment on the ground floor was not nearly in the
same condition.
Both apartments have the same characteristic small entrance, with little space for outerwear. The
two kitchens where in acceptable condition. However, there was no cooker hood in the smallest apart-
ment. The single room apartment had a combined toilet and bathroom, while the larger apartment had
toilet and shower in two separate adjacent rooms. Both apartments showed signs of moisture damages
in relation with the bathroom. However, the moisture issues in the single room apartment where critical,
where the walls and flooring in the bathroom showed serious decay due to moisture. In the ground floor
apartment, the moisture issues in the bathroom was mainly regarding traces of mould, also indicating
insufficient ventilation and removal of damp. Additionally, there was moisture formation along some
internal walls in the ground floor apartment. All internal load bearing walls located on the ground
floor had cold surfaces near the flooring due to insufficient insulation of the ground floor. The parquet
flooring in the ground floor also showed damages indicating moisture exposure.

4.3 Hazardous materials
35
4.3
Hazardous materials
Due to the time period in which the apartment buildings where constructed, the existence of hazardous
materials is probable [11]. In relation to the planned demolition of the Paamaap Kuua apartment blocks
Rambøll was hired to conduct an environmental examination of the blocks to analyze the presence of
hazardous materials. Block 7, 8, 10, 13 and 14 where examined individually and for each building an
environmental assessment report of hazardous materials was produced. The study was conducted in
preparation for demolition of the apartment blocks and to evaluate the waste handling strategy. The
reports contain a mapping of hazardous materials including asbestos, PCB, chlorinated paraffins and
eight metals (arsenic, lead, cadmium, chromium, copper, mercury, nickel and zinc).
For understanding the extent of presence of hazardous materials, these reports have been examined.
As mentioned, the reports where produced in relation with the planned demolition, and therefore, they
do not qualify the effects on the current living conditions due to presence of hazardous materials.
This section consists of three subsections describing the occurrence of hazardous materials (respec-
tively asbestos, PCB and metals) followed by a summary of the main conclusions drawn. Each section
consists of an introduction to what the hazardous material is and why it is a environmental or health
hazard. Additionally the handling and presence of material in Paamaap Kuua is described. A detailed
overview of the hazardous materials present in the buildings is found in Appendix A.1. In the following
the main conclusions based on that will be presented.
4.3.1
Asbestos
Use of asbestos in the danish building industry is present in the time period from the end of 1920’s to
the start 1970’s [43]. Buildings from after this period do not contain asbestos due to the regulations
established against its use. However, there is a risk of asbestos in buildings from before this period,
due to possible renovations or repairs conducted during the asbestos period. Use of asbestos peaked
from 1952 to 1972 where use of asbestos in tiles, tile adhesive and sealant grew. Additionally, due to
its ability to withstand wear, use of materials containing asbestos was commonly used in apartment
housing and public institutions [42]. Building materials used in the Greenlandic building industry was
imported from Denmark, therefore the same tendencies of asbestos in the Danish building industry
apply for the Greenlandic.
Asbestos is a group of natural occurring minerals that can be split into fibres. The fibers have the
characteristics as highly flexible material with high strength that are highly resistant to fire and wear.
These unique qualities are the reason for the great extent of application in the building industry [43].
Materials containing asbestos are in general more durable and their prolonged lifespan contribute to
still being widely present in building mass, despite the several years since the injunction against use of
the material was established.
When working with asbestos and materials containing asbestos, asbestos dust may develop. This
dust is very dangerous to inhale. The structure of the dust fibers are so fine that when inhaled they
==PAGE:56==
36
4 Case study: Concrete apartment buildings
can penetrate into the smallest branches of the lungs. The dust can not be removed and diseases may
develop from the asbestos fibers.
Handling of asbestos
Asbestos is only dangerous when airborne, meaning asbestos fibers that are bound in a material or
enclosed does not pose any harm to the surroundings. However, one must take precaution if a material
containing asbestos is damaged, perforated or an encasement of loose fibers is damaged. There are
three main strategies for handling exposed asbestos; sealing (for example paint on perforated asbestos
plate), enclose the exposed area or complete removal [42].
It is prohibited to reuse materials containing asbestos [3]. Therefore, if materials or building parts
containing asbestos is removed, it is not allowed to reuse this and must be sent to the landfill. The
environmental protection agency classifies asbestos waste in two categories [42]:
• Dust provoking asbestos waste is classified as dangerous waste (e.g. asbestos dust from fibers,
filters, soft ceiling and wall plates, and damaged roof plates)
• Non dusty asbestos waste is classifies as non-dangerous waste (e.g.
non-damaged asbestos
plates)
Handling of asbestos waste is determined on a municipal level and the handling is dependant on the
capacity of the specific waste deposit site [42]. Asbestos dust and dust provoking asbestos waste is to be
handled as hazardous waste. This must be transported under moist conditions and have a sealed and
enclosed packaging clearly stating asbestos waste. Asbestos waste not provoking dust does not require
special packaging [11]. However, as Sisimiut does not have the appropriate facilities for depositing non
dusty asbestos, all waste containing asbestos must be transported to Denmark.
Asbestos in Paamaap Kuua
The main observations on asbestos withdrawn from the environmental report conducted by Rambøll
was that all facade plates on the Paamaap Kuua apartment blocks are eternit plates reinforced with
asbestos fibres. Additionally, the divider plates between all private balcony contain asbestos. In several
of the blocks (three out of five examined blocks) the stairwell had asbestos plates on the wall. There
was primarily only non dust provoking asbestos in the buildings. A possible handling of these eternit
plates in relation with a renovation, will demand that they are transported to Denmark for deposit.
4.3.2
PCB
Approximately 45% of the Greenlandic building mass was constructed in the time period 1950 - 1970,
the period in which PCB was widely used in the building industry [17]. PCB is very present in the
Greenlandic building mass and due to the time of construction it is very relevant to assume presence
of PCB in the apartment blocks on Paamaap Kuua.
==PAGE:57==
4.3 Hazardous materials
37
Due to its many qualities as fire retardant and softener PCB was widely used in the building industry
from approx. 1950 til regulations made it illegal in 1977 [17]. Typical building materials where PCBs
are found are:
• Elastic joint sealants between walls, in between concrete elements and surrounding doors and
windows
• Sealants in windows
• Paints
• Flooring coating (epoxy)
• Adhesives
PCB evaporates from the source polluting the indoor air. According to the Danish Health Authority
the limit for acceptable PCB-concentration limit in indoor air is < 300 ng PCB/m3 [46].
If PCB
concentrations are below this level the Danish Health Authority does not consider the PCB to have any
health impacts. Concentrations above 3.000 ng PCB/m3 is considered to pose a large threat to health
safety, and immediate removal of PCB source or control the PCB exposure is recommended. It is not
recommended to be exposed to concentrations between the two limits for extended time periods, and
it is recommended to initiate processes to lower the concentration to under 300 ng PCB/m3. However,
according to the danish PCB-guide it is considered that only a minor fraction of the existing building
mass, from the relevant time period, have PCB concentrations in the indoor air surpassing the Danish
Health Authorities threshold for acceptable concentrations [47].
According to a study on the relation between PCB concentrations in source and indoor air concen-
trations Grontmij2013, an analysis of the PCB concentration in the primary sources is not sufficient to
evaluate the PCB concentration in the adjacent building materials or indoor air. A relation was seen
between elevated air concentrations in buildings with high PCB concentrations in sealants (> 100.000
mg/kg) and in buildings with PCB in indoor paint (>5.000 mg/kg). However, there was also seen
several instances where high indoor air concentration was measured in buildings with relatively low
concentrations of PCB in paint or flooring, where it is difficult to determine the cause to high PCB
elevations.
Being exposed to PCB has not been seen to pose any acute diseases in humans, however a continuous
exposure through many years accumulates in the body and a series of health issues are related to long
term exposure [11].
Handling of PCB
Rambølls environmental report states waste handling measures for different waste fragments according
to different PCB concentrations and regulations. The full table describing all waste handling measures
can be found in appendix A.1.2. The concentration of PCB in the waste fragments determine if the
==PAGE:58==
38
4 Case study: Concrete apartment buildings
waste is classified as clean, can be incinerated locally in Greenland or if the waste has to be handled at
an authorized waste handling unit in Denmark.
When renovating a PCB contaminated building where the indoor air concentrations exceed the
regulations there are two main strategies for reducing the PCB concentrations in the indoor air; reduction
of the PCB source or exposure control [2]. Reduction of sources is to remove completely or modify the
source, thus reduce the emission and the PCB-concentration will reduce as a result. Exposure control
are other initiatives taken to reduce the concentration without removing the source (enclose source,
ventilation, purification of air, cleaning, temperature reduction).
PCB in Paamaap Kuua
From Rambøll’s assessment it is seen that there is a presence of PCB in the buildings, primarily at
very low concentrations (around or less than 1 mg/kg).
Therefore, PCB concentrations is not the
critical factor classifying waste as hazardous to be handled at an authorized waste handling unit in
Denmark. This is assumed for the blocks on Paamaap Kuua based on the available environmental
reports. As mentioned, the environmental assessment of Paamaap Kuua was conducted in relation to
the demolition. Therefore, there is no studies on the PCB concentrations in regards to the indoor air
quality. To evaluate the current living conditions regarding PCB concentrations, tests of the indoor air
must be conducted to assess whether the presence of PCB is enough for creating elevated indoor air
concentrations. As mentioned, only a minor fraction of the existing building mass from the time period
is considered to have PCB concentrations in indoor air above the limit. However, even though the
measured PCB concentration in Paamaap Kuua are generally low, this is not necessarily an indication
of no risk of PCB in the indoor air.
4.3.3
Metals
Metals have been widely used in the building industry for many years and are still used today. However,
some metals are less favourable for the environment than others. When considering hazardous metals in
the building industry there are eight metals which are frequently discussed. These are lead, cadmium,
chromium, copper, nickel, zinc, arsenic and mercury [11].
Metals are mainly classified as hazardous when having to be handled as waste. The metals have
different characteristics and have different reasons to why they are classified as dangerous and some
metals are more hazardous than others.
Handling of metals
As mentioned, metals are mainly an issue when concerning waste treatment. In Appendix A.1.1 limit
values for waste classification of different metal concentrations are stated. They are classified as either
clean waste, contaminated waste or hazardous waste. Hazardous waste required handling at authorized
==PAGE:59==
4.3 Hazardous materials
39
waste handling plant. Contaminated waste may be incinerated locally if suitable for incineration. How-
ever, if the contaminated waste requires to be deposited, this must be done in Denmark, as Sisimiut
does not currently have the appropriate facilities for depositing contaminated waste. Clean waste can
be freely deposited.
Metals in Paamaap Kuua
The environmental assessment conducted by Rambøll is based on samples on paint, varnish and flooring
tested for content of hazardous metals. The report reveals a large presence of metals in the apartment
blocks with concentrations resulting in waste classified as hazardous, which must be sent to Denmark
for handling. Additionally, there is a presence of contaminated waste, that may be handled in Sisimiut,
if the waste is incinerable. Contaminated waste that is not incinerable must be sent to Denmark for
handling.
Based on the environmental assessment, the main hazardous waste fragments due to metal content
is listed in the following:
• Metal paint on staircase railings and metal plate above foundation
• Red and black wooden paint on window frames, doors and facade cladding
• White paint on wooden and concrete balcony railings
• Paint on ceilings and walls (indoors)
• Painted wood (indoors)
The aforementioned waste fragments requires handling in Denmark in most of the cases. The report
recommends that paint on hard surfaces (as concrete) to be cleaned off and disposed separately, where
the cleaned concrete is no longer classified as hazardous. Paint on soft material (as wood) and painted
metal should be handled in Denmark with paint on.
4.3.4
Main conclusion on hazardous materials
The environmental survey conducted by Rambøll in relation to the demolition of Paamaap Kuua gives
a good indication of the presence of hazardous materials in the apartment blocks considering waste
handling. From the report it is seen that there is a presence of hazardous materials in the Paamaap
Kuua buildings. Appendix A.1.3 summarizes all coincidences with hazardous materials in the blocks
categorized as non hazardous waste, contaminated waste for incineration/landfill or hazardous waste.
The table provides an overview over the distribution and quantity of instances for all examined blocks.
Deviance between the buildings was noticed.
The analysis of PCB in Paamaap Kuua showed low concentrations of PCB in the source samples.
However, an analysis of the PCB concentrations in the indoor air must be conducted to verify accept-
able indoor air conditions regarding PCB. It was seen that all eternit plates on the building envelope
==PAGE:60==
40
4 Case study: Concrete apartment buildings
contained asbestos. These are classified as non-dangerous waste, but must still be sent to Denmark
for handling if removed as Sisimiut does not have appropriate waste handling facilities for depositing
asbestos plates. Test sampling for hazardous metals showed that high metal concentration content was
the primary reason for waste fragments classified as hazardous. High metal concentrations was found
in various paints. This includes paint on window frames, doors, wooden cladding, balcony and staircase
railings, as well as painted walls and ceilings in the apartments. The main hazardous materials are
summarized on the next page.
Based on the available information, a renovation of Paamaap Kuua is considered possible despite the
presence of hazardous materials, if appropriate precautions are made. Many of the building elements
containing hazardous materials are much likely going to be replaced in a renovation proposal for other
reasons. This includes the non bearing facade elements that contains many hazardous metals, and is
additionally thermally outdated. However, the painted concrete needs to be cleaned off if contaminated,
if the concrete has to be reused. Nevertheless this and the other mentioned initiatives needs to be done
if the buildings are being demolished anyway, as the hazardous materials has to be separated. Thus
the handling of hazardous materials in a renovation project will not exceed the needed handling in a
demolition of the apartment blocks.
It is important to state that this assessment was based on the environmental survey on the buildings
in Paamaap Kuua. The main conclusions from these reports may not be extrapolated to other buildings
in other cities. Due to the time period of construction, it is known that use of hazardous materials was
very common in the industry and similar apartment buildings in other cities may have been built using
different material. Therefore, tendencies seen in Sisimiut may not comply with tendencies in other
cities.

4.4
Themography analysis
In this section the results from the thermography investigation will be presented.
The aim of this
inspection is to get an understanding of the thermal performance of the apartment blocks, including
identifying possible thermal bridges and other defects influencing the thermal performance.
The method and the input parameters will be described briefly, which will be followed by the results.
The results from the external building parts will be shown, followed by results from the two internal
investigations of apartment 0.13 and 2.06 in Block 8. All the results shown in the following are also
found in Appendix E with full size pictures, measured values together with the original picture of the
analyzed areas.
4.4.1
Method and input parameters
Thermography is a method where the temperature of an object is analyzed, by measuring the infrared
radiation emitted from that object. The method can be performed on buildings, showing the surface
temperatures of the different building parts. This gives an insight to where the heat fluxes occur in the
building and where thermal bridges are located.
The surface temperature of the building envelope is, in addition to the heat transfer from the inside
of the building and the ambient temperature, very dependent on the solar radiation that is hitting
the surface. If the sun is hitting a surface, it might get very hot and thereby making it difficult to
identify the heat fluxes from inside the building. Therefore, the external thermal inspection of the
building blocks was carried out early in the morning. The inspection was carried out on the 21st of
April at around 6 am just after sunrise. Furthermore, the sky were overcast, giving optimal conditions
for thermal inspection of the apartment blocks.
The thermal inspection was made using an infrared camera that measures the infrared radiation
emitted from the surfaces. Since the radiation is a function of the objects surface temperature, the
camera is able to measure the surface temperature. Other parameters also influence the surface tem-
perature that the camera calculates, such as emissivity, reflected temperature from the surroundings,
atmospheric temperature and distance to the object, whereas the two first mentioned are the most
important [23]. The emissivity is set to 0.95, which is common for many building surfaces such as
concrete, many types of paints etc. The reflected temperature is estimated by the assumptions of the
surrounding surface temperatures. The radiators were set to max in apartment 0.13 meaning the in-
door air temperature were very high and significantly higher than in apartment 2.06. Some of the input
parameters are seen in Table 4.2.
Table 4.2: Input parameters for infrared camera
External
Internal, 0.13
Internal, 2.16
Emissivity
0.95
0.95
0.95

4.4 Themography analysis

4.4.2
Thermography equipment
The tool used in this thermal investigation, is the FLIR E6 Infrared Camera by FLIR Systems. FLIR
Systems is a well know manufacturer of infrared cameras for thermal imaging with several years of
experience, and is on of the leading companies in the world within the field [22]. The FLIR E6 Infrared
Camera is a hand held camera, thus making it easy to carry it with you.
FLIR Systems has invented a technology that is called FLIR MSX (Multi-Spectral Dynamic Imag-
ing), which makes it easier to interpret the results. The Infrared Camera takes both a thermal image and
a original photo, and blend these to create an image that is easier to interpret, without compromising
with the thermal results. The thermal images shown in the analysis is Thermal MSX images.
4.4.3
External analysis
Facade
On the following thermography images, a part of the facade of Block 7, 8 and 9 is seen.
Looking
closely at Block 8 and 9, it is seen that the critical areas in the facade are the windows, cantilevers
underneath balconies and joints between the facade and load bearing walls. The high heat transfer
through windows are, not surprisingly, because of the relatively high u-value of the windows.
The
thermal bridges between the facade elements are caused by the inner concrete load bearing walls that
join the outer facade. Because of reduced insulation in this joint the heat from inside transfers through
here at a greater extent. The cantilevers who support the balconies are a common thermal bridge since
it penetrates the building envelope, thus making it possible for the heat to transfer easily to the outside.
Furthermore, the heat loss through the fresh air inlets can be seen on Figure 4.37 as dots in the facade.
Figure 4.37: Facade of Block 8
Figure 4.38: Facade of Block 9
==PAGE:64==
44
4 Case study: Concrete apartment buildings
Figure 4.39: Facade of Block 7
On Figure 4.39 the first thing that comes to mind is the relatively high temperatures of the basement
wall of Block 7. This building is different from the other apartment blocks, since some of the ground
level is unused and instead has concrete walls that fits to the uneven terrain (see original photo in
Appendix E).
The reason for this relatively big heat loss is not due to the outer wall being directly connected to
the slab on the 1st floor. As explained in Section 4.1 Structure and layout, the cast concrete around
the edges of the building is not in direct contact with the slabs as the load bearing inner walls are, but
are insulated in the joints. Since the temperature on the concrete wall is distributed uniformly on the
surface and not from the top near the slab, it suggests that the heat is coming from the space inside.
The same issue applies for the foundation, where the big heat loss is clearly seen on Figure 4.37 near
the foundation. Since the ground floor slab is very poorly insulated the spaces underneath the building
is heated from the rooms above. The issue might be even worse in Block 7, since the heated space is
underneath apartments on the 1st floor, and the original drawings shows only insulation underneath
slabs at ground level. It is however uncertain if the 1st floor slab has been insulated from underneath
in this particularly case.
Figure 4.40 shows a close up of a Block 9 facade, and the thermal bridges is clearly seen here.
The mean surface temperature of the window element and the insulated facade is 1.8°C and -1.3°C
respectively, showing a relatively big difference. On Figure 4.41 the hot and cold spot is shown for an
area on the facade of Block 8. The coldest spot (-3.4°C) is not surprisingly located in an insulated area,
where the hot spot (1.0°C) is located around the cantilever. Furthermore, it is seen that the doors are
a big thermal bridge. A close up of the basement of Block 7, where the high heat transfer through
the basement walls is evident, can be seen on Figure 4.42. The thermal bridges around the cantilevers
under the balconies are also seen clearly here and on Figure 4.43.

Internal analysis
In this chapter the results of the thermography analysis of the two inspected apartments (0.13 and 2.06
in Block 8) will be presented. In contrary to the outdoor thermal investigations, a warm spot usually
means the heat is kept inside, whereas a cold spot means heat is transferred to the outside.
Both apartment 0.13 and 2.06 were heated, however apartment 0.13 had the radiators turned fully
on meaning the indoor air temperature were very high and significantly higher than in apartment 2.06,
as explained. That can also be seen on the following results, where the surface temperatures in general
are higher in apartment 0.13. Thermal bridges and tendencies are however clear in both cases.
Apartment 0.13

a significant difference. The same is seen on Figure 4.49, where the external door is analyzed. The
coldest spots are around the edges, where the cold spot is down to 8.2°C.

4 Case study: Concrete apartment buildings
4.5
Consideration on challenges and potentials in Paamaap Kuua
Based on the aforementioned, it has been found that there is a great potential in renovating the apart-
ment blocks of Paamaap Kuua. Almost the entire load bearing concrete structure has been protected
from the outdoors since the internal walls and slabs constitutes the structural system. Some repairs is
much likely inevitable, such as to repair and enclose exposed reinforcement in the load bearing gable
wings. As explained, this and the overall structural capacities of the existing concrete needs to be
investigated before a renovation can be realized. Structural analysis is, as mentioned, not included
in this project, and as a result it has been assumed that the load bearing concrete structure can be
reused, without too much repair. This assumption is a key prerequisite of the following work, but has
been found reasonable based on dialogues with competent people familiar with the apartment blocks of
Paamaap Kuua.
Additionally, it has been found that the old facade of the existing buildings should be replaced, since
it is thermally outdated. This is relatively simple since the facade is non bearing. Further investigations
on handling of hazardous materials must be assessed regarding renovation of Paamaap Kuua. Despite
the presence of hazardous materials, it is assumed that renovation of the building should be plausible
and will not necessarily lead to additional expenses for handling compared to demolition based on the
available knowledge.
While dealing with the facade, it would much likely be beneficial to remove the interior as well and
only keeping the the concrete structures. Some of the interior could probably be reused, but this requires
a thorough analysis that is not only based on the condition of two apartments, and this discussion is
thus not included in this thesis. The investigated proposals are focusing on the building envelope and
the exact construction of the interior is therefore less significant.
One of the important aspects that needs to be handled is the occurrence of the many thermal
bridges in the apartment blocks. Especially the cantilevers is a severe thermal bridge. In the following
renovation proposals the cantilevers have been kept. As it has been wished not to interfere with the
concrete structural system too much, the renovation proposals investigates, among other things, how
the cantilevers can be included in a thermally improved building.
All the aforementioned, combined with the overall great potential in the existing plan solutions in
the apartments, makes a renovation case interesting. Additionally, it is found beneficial to reuse the
existing concrete load bearing structure, that otherwise would have to be constructed once again, if
the existing buildings are bearing teared down and replaced by new ones. Especially in Greenland,
where the transportation of construction materials are very troublesome, reusing the existing structural
system seems like an obvious choice. Furthermore, a renovation of the buildings gives the possibility of
utilizing the existing infrastructure as well as the piping system for water and sewage already supporting
the buildings.
==PAGE:71==
CHAPTER 5
Energy study
In this chapter, the energy performance of the existing apartment blocks and possible renovation solu-
tions are being investigated. The renovation solutions are based on the findings from the case study,
as well as findings from the energy simulations. The renovation solutions are referred as scenarios and
will be explained in the following before the energy analyses are presented. All of the scenarios should
not necessarily be seen as final renovation proposals, as some are a result of the process and shows
important tendencies and gives a better understanding of which initiatives which are effective.
The energy analysis is divided into three parts. First the heat loss due to the thermal bridges will
be investigated for different scenarios using a FEA (Finite Element Analysis) software. This part will
give an understanding of the existing thermal bridges in the apartment blocks as well as how different
solutions influences this. Furthermore, these calculations is the foundation for the next analysis, where
the overall energy consumption of the different scenarios are being analyzed using a energy simulation
software. The aim of this is to see how different initiatives such as improving the facade, eliminating
thermal bridges and implementing a new ventilation system, will affect the energy consumption.
The last analysis in this chapter is investigating how the thermal bridges are influencing the indoor
surface temperatures, and how this affects the risk of condensation and possible mould growth.
5.1
Description of analyzed scenarios
This section will give an overview of the different scenarios that will be analyzed in the following
sections. The different scenarios are a result of the design process, where the different results of the
energy performance was a key factor when developing the design. In the following, the building form
of the different scenarios will be presented. In addition to the building form, some scenarios will be
investigated in respect to other parameters, such as different ventilation systems. This will be explained
in Section 5.3 - Energy Performance.
The first scenario that is being analyzed is the existing case, which has already been presented in
Chapter 4 - Case study. Block 8 is used as the reference apartment block in the following analysis, and
a model of the building is seen on Figure 5.1. The first set of analyses are investigating the impact
of replacing the old facade with a new one. The old facade that is not load bearing is removed, while
the load bearing concrete inner walls, slabs and cantilevers are kept, and a new thermally improved
facade with new windows is placed in the same location as the old facade. The location of windows
is identical as well. This solution can be seen as the one that intervenes the least with the existing
==PAGE:72==
52
5 Energy study
building. Three new facade systems will be investigated, with 200mm, 300mm and 400mm insulation
thickness respectively (in Section 5.1.1 details of the analyzed facade systems and other building parts
as well as details of important thermal bridges will be shown). These analyses are made to see the effect
of only improving the facade and windows, while no major intervention with the design has been made.
The thermal bridges are expected to still have an significant impact on the energy performance in this
case.
As the thermography analysis shows, one of the biggest thermal challenges with the existing apart-
ment blocks, is the thermal bridges in the cantilevers and the gable wings. Therefore, the next two
scenarios has been designed in such a way that the thermal bridges in the facade are eliminated. Models
of the two scenarios can be seen on Figure 5.2 and Figure 5.3. As for the aforementioned solutions,
the old facade is torn down while the load bearing concrete structure is kept. In Proposal 1 the new
facade is moved out to the outer side of the cantilevers, which eliminates the thermal bridges. New
balconies matching the existing ones in size are then created on the exterior side of new envelope, and
are supported by an exterior system of load bearing columns. The staircases are included as living
space, and are replaced by exterior staircases at the gable ends. The existing floor plan of Block 8, and
the floor plan of Proposal 1 is seen on Figure 5.4 and Figure 5.5.
Proposal 2 is similar to Proposal 1. The facade is moved out so the cantilevers is enclosed by the
new envelope. However between some of the large facade elements, the facade is kept at the original
position, as shown in Figure 5.6. In addition, new stairwell towers have been created and as a result the
size of the access balconies has been increased. The reason for this is to improve the daylight conditions
in the apartments, as well as creating more privacy by avoiding people walking past the windows of
their neighbours. Furthermore, the expression of the building form is changed from the very straight
lines that dominate the existing building form. The design process that is related to the functionality
of the proposals will however be discussed in Chapter 6 - Concept and design development.

Details of building elements
On the following page details of important building elements (regarding energy performance) from the

are presented in Section 4.1.5. The drawings are describing the principle of the analysis, and are not

==PAGE:76==
