A) On Drawing A the principle for the new non bearing building facade is seen. As for the existing
case the facade consists of a wooden framework with insulation in between. The core of the wall
system has an insulation thickness of 100 mm, 200 mm and 300 mm respectively, where a thinner
insulation layer of 50 mm is added on both sides. A vapor barrier has been placed in between the
insulation core and the inner insulation layer. Furthermore, a 9 mm thick CEMBRIT Extreme
windstopper has been implemented to ensure an airtight envelope which is important in the tough
arctic climate.
B) The principle of the construction of the gable is seen on drawing B. Again the insulation core
thickness changes from 100 mm, 200 mm and 300 mm. The construction has a 50 mm insulation
layer on the outside whereas the existing 2x50 mm insulation layers in framework is kept on the
inside. This system has however been changed in the final renovation proposal, since it has been
found that a solution where the existing insulation is removed would be more pragmatic (this
will be elaborated in see Section 6.4 - Buildability). The U-value is however identical for the two
modifications, and will therefore practically not influence the energy performance. Compared to
the facade system, no vapor barrier is needed, since the concrete load bearing wall acts as a vapor
barrier [5]. This is valid if the concrete layer is continuous with no gaps, which is realistic in this
case as all concrete are cast. The aforementioned is also valid for the concrete slabs and thus a
vapor barrier is not needed there [37].
C) The principle of the re-insulated first floor slab, that creates a new envelope is shown on drawing
C. Insulation in framework is added beneath the slab, and the relatively large distance between
the slabs of of 2.68 m ensures that the ground floor is still useful for other activities even though
it is left unheated (this will be elaborated in Section 6.2.2). In the following analysis the load
bearing inner walls in the ground floor will be insulated with a 50 mm insulation layer to see the
impact on the thermal bridge. The insulation distance will be changed as seen on the right side
of drawing C. This will be elaborated in the analysis.
D) Drawing D shows a detail of the short gable wing. The impact of the different wall systems on
the thermal bridge will be analyzed in the following. The construction of the facade and the gable
will change according to drawing A and B (here the drawing is shown for the case with a 200 mm
insulation core).
E) For the proposals analyzed in the following, the roof construction is similar to the existing case,
with a cold attic. The insulation thickness of the roof slab is increased as shown on drawing E.

performance.

5.2
Heat flow through thermal bridges
Thermal bridges are the section of a construction significantly worse insulated than the rest of the
structure [13]. Thermal bridges arise in junctions between building components and where the structure
of the building component changes, causing the envelope, with an otherwise uniform thermal resistance,
to be fully or partially penetrated by a building part with another thermal conductivity [31]. Thermal
bridges cause a change in the heat flow rate and a change in the internal surface temperature.
Thermal bridges are very important to include when calculating the total heat loss, as they may
contribute with a large share of the total heat loss for the building [6].
In DS 418 Calculation of
heat loss from buildings values for frequently occurring thermal bridges in common constructions are
stated. However, the heat flows through thermal bridges can also be determined through numerical
calculation methods. Since the analyzed apartment blocks in this thesis contains many thermal bridges,
the impact of these will be investigated in detail. Additionally, due to the low temperatures in Greenland,
determining the actual heat loss through thermal bridges is important, as you are dealing with a greater
proportions of heat loss. In this thesis this assessment is carried out in accordance with the European
Standard EN ISO 10211:2008 for thermal bridges in building construction. The standard describes
the method for precisely determining the heat flow rate and internal surface temperatures in thermal
bridges, and will be explained in detail later in this section. First the theory and methodology behind
this will be explained, wheres the results are presented hereafter.
5.2.1
General considerations on heat flow through thermal bridges
When considering the energy performance of a building, one of the key parameters is the thermal
transmission of the envelope. The thermal transmittance of a building part is given by the U-value,
which depends on the thermal resistance of the different material layers in the construction as well as
the surface resistances [13]:
1
U ′ = Rsi + Rse +

Ri

where

Rse
the external surface resistance [W/m2K]
Ri
the thermal resistance of the different layers [W/m2K]
n
number of layers in the construction
The thermal resistance depends on the thickness of the examined construction layer and the thermal
conductivity for that layer, as seen in Equation 5.2.
R = d

(5.2)
==PAGE:79==

where
d

λ
the thermal conductivity of the material [W/mK]
In this thesis the U-value is not corrected for potential gaps in the insulation layer, wall ties and
other that might penetrate the insulation layer. In the following Ucorr, refers to the U-value corrected
for thermal bridges.
As mentioned thermal bridges can potentially impact the energy performance a lot, and if not
considered, the total heat loss may be greatly underestimated. Therefore, it must be included. This
can be done by either correcting the U-value to include the linear and point thermal transmittance or
by lastly adding the heat loss through thermal bridges to the total heat loss through the construction.
The U-value corrected for thermal bridges is given by Equation 5.3:

Ui describes the one dimensional heat transfer through a given area of the building envelope. How-
ever, one dimensional heat transfer can not be used to describe the heat loss through thermal bridges.
These calculations require a two or three dimensional heat flow analysis on the given construction. The
temperature distribution and three- and two-dimensional heat flows through solids can be calculated
using a FEA (Finite Element Analysis) simulation software.
This is done by dividing a 2D or 3D
geometry, representing the object of interest, into several cells (mesh), each with a number of bound-
ary points (nodes), and numerically calculating a system of equations in each node. In the following
key equations regarding this method will be presented, whereas the methodology regarding the Finite
Element Method will be described in Section 5.2.2.
==PAGE:80==
60
5 Energy study
In the next couple of presented equations, the thermal conductivity is given by k [W/mK], which
is identical to the aforementioned λ [W/mK] (λ is commonly used in Denmark and thus in DS 418).
The mathematical equation describing heat transfer in solids is:
ρCp
δT
δt −∇· (k∇T) = Q
(5.5)
where
ρ
density [kg/m3]
Cp
specific heat capacity [J/kg*K]
T
absolute temperature [K]
k
thermal conductivity [W/mK]
Q
heat source (or sink) [W/m3]
When considering steady-state problems the temperature is not time dependent. Therefore, the first
term can be removed, and it is seen that material properties such as density ρ and heat capacity Cp
does not influence the heat transfer in steady-state. The equation is then reduced to Equation 5.6. The
remaining term on the left side represents the conductive heat flux through the solids, also known as
Fourier’s law (q = −k∇T, where q is the local heat flux density [W/m2]) [10].
−∇· (k∇T) = Q
(5.6)
Also relevant to consider for this analysis is the heat flux conditions on the surface nodes on the
geometrical model. The heat flux conditions are governed by Equation 5.7. The term on the left side
describes the conductive heat flux and the term on the right describes the convection.
−n· (−k∇T) = h(Text −T)
(5.7)
where
n
normal vector of the boundary
h
heat transfer coefficient (h = 1
R) [W/m2K]
Text
exterior temperature
By applying the laws of energy conservation (∇q = 0) on the equation for heat transfer in solids
and taking into account the surface boundary conditions, a system of equations for each node in the
model is obtained. These can be used to calculate the temperature/heat flow at each node, from which
the temperature distribution and the heat flow in the solid are found.
From Equation 5.6 and 5.7 it is seen that the thermal conductivity of the material (k) influences the
heat flow through solids. Concrete has a thermal conductivity of 2.5 [W/mK] while modern insulation
can have a thermal conductivity of 0.035 [W/mK] [32], a substantially lower thermal conductivity. Thus,
it is not difficult to imagine the effect concrete, or another material with a poor thermal conductivity,
has on the thermal performance of a construction, when penetrating an otherwise well-insulated facade.
==PAGE:81==
5.2 Heat flow through thermal bridges
61
5.2.2
Prerequisites and basis of the heat flow analysis
In this section the basis of the heat flow analysis will be presented.
The software used, modelling
choices, simplifications and input values will be described in the following. Lastly the applied method
for calculating the thermal bridges will be explained thoroughly.
COMSOL Multiphysics
The software used to calculate the thermal bridges in this thesis is COMSOL Multiphysics. COMSOL
Multiphysics is a software environment for solving scientific and engineering problems, able to handle
multiphysics and coupling of physical phenomena. The software can calculate three-dimensional heat
transmittance in steady-state with high precision, and fulfills the requirements for being a validated
calculation method according to the European Standard EN ISO 10211:2008 for thermal bridges in
building constructions. Therefore, it has been chosen to use this software for determining the thermal
bridges in Paamaap Kuua. The 2D and 3D geometric modelling has been conducted in coherence with
ISO 10211, which will be elaborated later.
The heat flow in COMSOL Multiphysics is assessed using a Finite Element Method, where the
analyzed domain is split into smaller domains, so called mesh cells. This procedure (discretization)
allows the software to apply a series of equations to the mesh cells, that are solved for each node
between the cells. This means that numerical problems can be solved on a very detailed level for the
analyzed domain. The detail of the results depends then on the mesh size. The Heat Transfer Module
in COMSOL Multiphysics is used in the analysis.
Mesh
A 2D mesh and 3D mesh of an analyzed part of the thermal bridge around a cantilever is shown in
Figure 5.9. As seen COMSOL usually splits the geometry into triangular and tetrahedral mesh cells,
where the mesh is refined where different layers in the construction are joining. In the analysis the
fine-sized mesh setting has been used to achieve an appropriate mesh-resolution.
==PAGE:82==
62
5 Energy study
Figure 5.9:
Triangular (2D) and tetrahedral (3D) mesh elements mesh in geometrical model (COMSOL
Multiphysics)
Cut-off planes
The geometrical model needs to represent the thermal bridge concerned, and is delimited by cut-off
planes. The cut-off planes shall be adiabatic (i.e. zero heat flow). The requirements for placement of
the cut-off planes for modelling a thermal bridge (only considering thermal bridges not in contact with
ground conditions) are following:
• at a symmetry plane if this is less than dmin from central element
• minimum dmin from the central element if there are no symmetry planes nearer
where dmin is greater than 1 m and three times the thickness of the flanking element.
The flanking elements in the 3D geometrical model of the cantilever construction are the facade, the
slab and the internal wall. A cut-off distance of 1 m is the limiting value for the existing wall system,
as the thickness of the flanking elements are 0.16 m (facade), 0.12 m (slab) and 0.15 m (internal wall).
The cut-off planes are placed 1 m from the cantilever in all directions as shown in Figure 5.10. This
distance has been used for placing cut-off planes in all analysis conducted. This limit is applicable for
flanking elements with thickness <1/3 d.
However, for the thickest wall systems in the renovation proposals, the flanking element (representing
the wall) exceeds this limit. This was first discovered late in the process, and thus a re-modelling of
the scenarios has not been made. This minor source of error has been found to have very little impact
on the results. If the cut-off planes are adjusted to fit the aforementioned criteria, in the case with
the thickest wall system (400 mm insulation), the heat flow results changes between 0.6 % to 1.1 %
compared to the results of originally modelled geometry (see Appendix B.5.2 for calculation). This
relatively minor error will be further discussed in Section 7.1 - Discussion.
==PAGE:83==
5.2 Heat flow through thermal bridges
63

These axes may be drawn due to the adiabatic conditions between the four internal zones.

Homogeneous layers
A quasi-homogeneous layer is a layer which consists of two or more materials with different thermal
conductivities, but which can be considered as a homogeneous layer with a single thermal conductivity
[32]. The conditions for simplifying the layers in the geometrical modelling are:
• the layers must be located in a part of the construction, which becomes a flanking element after
simplification
• the thermal conductivity of the simplified layer may not exceed 1.5 times the lowest thermal
conductivity of the materials in the layer before simplification
For the geometric modelling, the quasi-homogeneous materials layers have been simplified into ho-
mogeneous layers (see Appendix B.3 and B.4 for calculation of thermal conductivity, k, for each layer).
This simplification complies with both of the before mentioned criteria. The simplified layers are flank-
ing elements in the assessed construction. Additionally, the thermal conductivity of the simplified layer
is less than 1.5 times the lowest thermal conductivity of the materials in the layer. The thermal con-
ductivity of the new insulation is 0.035 W/mK, whilst the thermal conductivity of the simplified layer
is calculated to 0.042 W/mK, thus less than 1.5 times greater.
To fully understand and justify the effects of the simplification, two geometrical models (with and
without simplification of quasi-homogeneous layers) is assessed using the same boundary conditions.
The following example is carried out for the thermal bridge in the cantilever for the existing case.
==PAGE:84==

on the temperature distribution, whilst the simplified model indicates a more uniform temperature

enhance the perception of the simplifications impact.

simplification in the geometrical modelling can be justified.

Design values
Design values of thermal conductivities of building materials have been taken from ISO 10456, DS418 or
found in technical data regarding specific products. All thermal conductivities used in this assessment
are listed in Appendix B.1. The thermal conductivity of quasi-homogeneous layers have been calculated
in accordance with ISO 10211.
Values for the surface resistances are according to DS 418 (see appendix B.2). Due to the ventilated
air cavity between the eternit plate and the internit wind barrier, the exterior surface resistance and
the thermal resistance for all layers between the air cavity and the exterior surface are to be replaced
by the internal surface resistance [13].
Furthermore vapor barriers and wind barriers under 1 mm thickness has been neglected, as non-
metallic layers with a thickness of not more than 1 mm may be ignored.
==PAGE:85==
5.2 Heat flow through thermal bridges
65
The external and internal boundary conditions are chosen according to the DS 418, the external
temperature of 0◦C and 20◦C for internal temperature.
As the thermal bridge is an expression of
heat flow dependent on the temperature difference between the boundary conditions it is not necessary
to calculate with local dimensioning temperatures. Therefore, it is chosen to use these values in the
standard when the heat flow of the thermal bridges are determined. However, when the actual indoor
surface temperatures are investigated, Greenlandic outdoor temperatures are used.
Simplification related to windows
The geometrical model has been simplified by not including the windows. Thermal bridges close to
each other might influence the heat flow. However, this effect has not been included in the analysis.
5.2.3
Procedure for determining linear and point thermal transmittance
In the following section the method for determining the thermal transmittance is described. The key
equations when calculating the linear thermal transmittance and the point thermal transmittance is
based on the method described in ISO 10211. For reference, the method will be applied on calculating
the thermal transmittance of the cantilever, as the analysis includes both types of thermal transmittance;
the point thermal transmittance related to the cantilever and the liner thermal loss due to the concrete
slabs and concrete inner walls. It should be noted that the procedure for calculating the linear thermal
transmittance in the foundation and the gable wings is identical to the procedure when determining
the linear thermal transmittance in the concrete slabs and concrete inner walls.
The linear thermal transmittance, Ψ [W/mK], is the heat flow rate in steady state divided by length
and temperature difference one either side of the thermal bridged The linear heat transmittance for
thermal bridges in constructions is found by:
1. Calculating the total two-dimensional heat flow through the thermal bridge and 1 m of the adjacent
construction
2. Divide with the difference between interior and exterior temperature
3. Subtract the one-dimensional heat flow through the same construction
and is given by the equation:

where

is the thermal coupling coefficient determined by a 2-D calculation separating the two thermal

The point thermal transmittance, χ [W/K], is the heat flow rate divided by temperature difference,
through a central element which can not be determined as thermal transmission over length or area. In
this case this is represented by the cantilever. The point thermal transmittance can be determined by:
1. Calculating the total three-dimensional heat flow through the thermal bridge and 1 m of the
adjacent construction
2. Divide with the difference between interior and exterior temperature
3. Subtract the one-dimensional heat flow through the same construction
4. Subtract the total linear transmittance through the same construction using equation 5.8

Applied procedure for determining linear and point thermal transmittance in a cantilever
In the following the applied procedure for determining linear and point thermal transmittance in a
cantilever will be presented. The existing case is taken as a reference, and the full calculations can be
found in Appendix B.5.1. The calculation of the linear transmittance in the gable wing and the ground
floor slab for the existing case is included in the Appendix as well (Appendix B.5.2).
Using COMSOL Multiphysics, the 2-dimensional flow rate is determined.
By dividing with the
temperature difference between the two boundary conditions the thermal coupling coefficient from the
two-dimensional calculation (L2D) is found. This was conducted for thermal transmittances through
the facade/internal wall section and facade/slab section. The flow rate through the internal wall/slab

one zone, therefore the length of linear loss must be the sum of the lengths for each zone. The length
of the linear loss relates to outer measurements.
χ = L3D −Uy,z· Ay,z −Ψy· ly −Ψz· lz
(5.10)
As mentioned in chapter 4.1, the width of one facade element is either 2.7 or 3.6 m. To evaluate
how much the cantilever contributes to the total heat flow, one facade element is considered (in this
case 2.7 m wide and 2.8 m high facade element, where the impact of the cantilever is most significant).
The total point thermal transmittance calculated, is equivalent to the effect on one element as all four
corners of the element will be influenced by the cantilever. The results of the assessment will be stated
and discussed in the following section. See Appendix B.5.1 for all calculations.
==PAGE:89==
5.2 Heat flow through thermal bridges
69
5.2.4
Heat flow results
The results from the heat flow analysis related to the thermal bridges in the cantilever, gable wing and
foundation will be presented in the following. The results will be summarized in the end of this section.
Cantilever
In the following the results from the thermal bridge analysis of the cantilever will be presented and
discussed.
The existing facade composition and the three replacement proposals are modeled and
compared.
Table 5.2 lists the results from the heat flow analysis, where beside the U-value, the heat loss from
the linear thermal transmittance and the thermal point loss is compared. It is seen, that the linear
thermal transmittance is decreasing for an increasing insulation thickness. This is however not the case
for the point loss when comparing the existing wall system (with 125 mm insulation thickness) and the
proposal with 200 mm insulation thickness. In contrast, the linear thermal transmittance is decreased
quite significantly comparing the two scenarios. The reason for this is likely due to a minor change in
the position of the new facade system. The position of the new facade is moved slightly out compared to
the existing wall system. Thus the distance to the concrete slabs and internal walls are slightly different
for the two cases. Because of this, the linear thermal transmittance is decreased significantly when
comparing the existing case and the new facade with 200 mm insulation, while the point loss remains
identical. The decreased heat loss in the thermal bridges resulting from a bigger insulation thickness,
is thus only evident for the linear thermal transmittances in this case.
When looking at the other two new facade system (300 mm and 400 mm insulation thickness) both
the linear thermal transmittance and the point loss are slightly reduced when the amount of insulation
is increased. The thermal bridge is still present as the cantilever is not insulated and still penetrates
the facade. However, when the wall thickness increases, the shortest heat transfer distance is increased.
Thus, the heat transfer is reduced and the point thermal transmission value is improved.

Gable wing
In the following the thermal bridge in relation to the gable wing is investigated. A cross section of the
existing gable wing can be found in Section 5.1.1.
On Figure 5.15 the 2D heat flow analysis of the existing gable wing is seen.
On both the 157
cm and 97 cm wide gable wing it is very clear to see how the heat from inside transfers out through
the uninsulated concrete gable wing. Even though the width of the two scenarios varies, the result
shows that, the heat flow through the thermal bridges are almost identical, and the linear thermal
transmittance is calculated to be the same with a value of 0.27 W/mK. That correspond to an overall
contribution of 20 % when compared to the total heat loss of a 2.7 m facade element with an U-value
of 0.39 W/m2K.
Figure 5.15: 2D heat flow analysis of the thermal bridge in the gable wings which are 157 cm (left) and 97 cm
(right) wide respectively with indication of the minimum indoor surface temperature (COMSOL Multiphysics)
The aim of the following analysis is to analyze, how the three aforementioned wall constructions
will impact the thermal bridge in the gable wing. On Figure 5.16 the 2D heat flow analysis for the long
gable wing is shown for the three scenarios (all of the analysis is found in Appendix B.6).
In addition to the fact that the overall energy performance is improved for the scenarios with greater
insulation thickness, the insulation thickness of the facade also affects the thermal bridge. The greater
the insulation thickness, the greater is the distance from the inside to the outside, and the shortest way
for the heat to transfer in the thermal bridge is increased. However since the concrete gable wing still
penetrates the envelope the thermal bridge is still severe.
==PAGE:91==
5.2 Heat flow through thermal bridges
71
Figure 5.16: 2D heat flow analysis of the thermal bridge in the 157 cm gable wing for the scenarios with new
facade (200mm, 300mm and 400mm insulation) and insulated gable (COMSOL Multiphysics)
The next sets of analysis is made to show how an eventual insulation of the end of the gable wing
will affect the thermal bridge. On Figure 5.17, the case with a 200 mm insulated wall system is shown
for both the long and short gable wing with and without insulation on the gable wing end. While the
insulation of the gable wing end doesn’t really improve the thermal performance of the long gable wing,
that is not true for the short gable wing. For the long gable wing the extra insulation is relatively far
away from the thermal bridge which still leaves the gable wing exposed to the outdoors. However for
the small gable wing, the insulation on the gable wing end is closer to the thermal bridge and is thus
decreasing the heat loss through the thermal bridge.
Figure 5.17: 2D heat flow analysis showing how the thermal bridge in gable wing of 157 cm (left) and 97 cm
(right) will be affected by adding insulation on the end of the gable wing (COMSOL Multiphysics)
All of the analysis made are summarized in Table 5.3. In general it is seen that the linear thermal
transmittance, Ψ is not being affected much even though the U-value of the different wall systems is
improved. The linear thermal transmittance decreases only a bit with smaller U-value, which is simply
due to the fact that the insulation layer in the wall system are thicker for smaller U-value, leading to
a greater distance for the heat to transfer in the thermal bridge. When looking at the total heat loss
contribution for a 2.7 m wide facade element it is seen that a decrease in U-value just increases the
impact of the linear thermal transmittance, leading to almost an even heat loss contribution between
the U-value and the linear thermal transmittance for the best insulated wall system.

5.2 Heat flow through thermal bridges

Ground/1st floor slab
In this section the thermal bridges related to the ground floor and 1st floor slab is assessed. As explained
in Section 4.1, the foundation consists of concrete beams, that are resting on piles, which are mounted to
the bedrock beneath. On top of the beams the ground floor slab has been cast in concrete. Underneath
the slab is a crawl space, meaning that both the beams and slab are not in directly contact with the
ground. The slab towards the crawl space is insulated with 75 mm mineral wool batts, and the beams
are insulated on each side with 30 mm mineral wool batts. Detailed drawings of the construction can
be found in Section 4.1.5. On top of the concrete slab is the floor construction consisting of joist with
75 mm insulation in between.
Figure 5.18 shows a 2D heat flow analysis of a representative part of the ground floor slab. The
analysis shows that the temperature drop in the thin insulation layer on the outside of the concrete is
relatively little. The thin insulation layer can’t effectively prevent the heat from the inside to transfer
out. Furthermore it is seen that the concrete even furthest away from the inside is relatively warm,
showing that the heat is easily transferring through this part. The U-value of the slab itself is 0.30
W/m2K and from the 2D analysis the linear thermal bridge corresponding to the thermal bridge from
the beam is calculated to 0.53 W/mK. When looking at a 2.7 m wide slab element the corrected U-value
for the slab will be 0.49 W/m2K, where the linear thermal bridge contributes with 40% of the heat loss
through the slab, which is quite significant.
Figure 5.18: 2D heat flow analysis of the thermal bridge in the foundation, with indication of the minimum
indoor surface temperature (COMSOL Multiphysics)
Since the crawl space underneath the ground floor slab is inaccessible and a further re-insulation
would be almost impossible, it has been decided to re-insulate the 1st floor slab and create a basement
at ground level, as described earlier.
On Figure 5.19 four proposals of re-insulation of the 1st floor slab is seen. For all the proposals it
is seen that the extra amount of insulation helps significantly the heat from transferring out, and the
indoor surface temperatures is significantly increased. However, since the load bearing concrete inner
walls connects to the slab, there is potentially a great thermal bridge here. The first proposal is only the
==PAGE:94==
74
5 Energy study
insulation of the slab, where there is added 30 cm, 60 cm and full insulation respectively on the concrete
inner wall in the next three proposals. Not surprisingly the analysis shows, that more insulation on the
wall stops the heat flow through the thermal bridge better and better.
Figure 5.19: 2D heat flow analysis of the thermal bridge in the slab on 1st floor where the inner wall has 0
cm, 30 cm, 60 cm and full insulation respectively (COMSOL Multiphysics)
In Table 5.5, the results of the aforementioned analysis are shown. The U-value and the correspond-
ing linear thermal transmittance, Ψ is shown for every analysis, together with the contribution of the
different heat losses when determining the total heat loss for a 2.7 m wide slab element. Finally the
corrected U-value, when taking the linear thermal transmittance into account, is shown.
As seen, the U-value decreases significantly from the existing value when adding more insulation in
the proposal. However the linear thermal transmittance is still very evident, especially in the scenario
with no insulation of the wall.
In this case the linear thermal transmittance accounts for 63 % of
the total heat loss in the slab, where this is only 40 % in the existing scenario. The linear thermal
transmittance drops when adding more and more insulation on the wall, but even in the case with the
full insulated wall the linear thermal transmittance accounts for almost half of the total heat loss. In
this case the corrected U-value drops to 0.18 W/m2K. In the following proposals this solution has been
chosen.

5.3 Energy performance

5.3
Energy performance
In this section the energy performance of the different scenarios will be analyzed. This will give an
overview of which renovation initiatives that is effective in relation to the energy performance.
In
addition this will lead to two renovation proposals, where the functionality of the design will be discussed
in the next chapter, Chapter 6 - Concept and design development.
In the following general considerations on energy consumption is presented first, followed by an
explanation of how the models is set-up, where also the key input parameters are described.
The
results of the analysis will then be presented in the end.
5.3.1
General considerations on energy consumption
In the following the most important aspects of a buildings energy consumption will be explained briefly.
This includes a presentation of a few of the general equations that is important when calculating the
energy consumption of a building. This will give an overview of the most relevant parameters in relation
to the energy performance of a building.
Generally, the energy consumption of a building can be divided into the energy need for electricity
and the energy need for heat. The energy need for electricity is usually little compared to the energy
need for heat, especially in cold regions, where the majority of the buildings energy is used to heat the
building. In the following analyses mechanical cooling of the building is excluded and electricity related
to lightning is not accounted for. Therefore the energy need for electricity only consists of electricity
for circulation pumps in heating and domestic hot water systems and electricity for ventilation systems
[24].
In general the energy need for heat consists of the heat needed to cover for heat loss from transmission
through the envelope and heat loss due to ventilation. Additionally the heat loss of hot water pipes and
hot water tanks outside the envelope and the heat needed for domestic hot water, should be accounted
for. As seen in Equation 5.11 solar heat gains and internal gains such as heat from occupants, equipment
and lightning is reducing the yearly energy need for heat.
The yearly energy need for heat, Qh consists of the following:
Qh = Qt + Qv + QDHW + Qloss −Qn
(5.11)
where
Qh
is the yearly energy need for heat
Qt
is the yearly heat loss due to transmission through the building envelope
Qv
is the yearly heat loss due to ventilation
QDHW
is the yearly energy need for domestic hot water
Qloss
is the yearly heat loss from hot water pipes and hot water tanks, that are outside the building
envelope
==PAGE:98==
78
5 Energy study
Qn
is the yearly heat gain from occupants, equipment and lightning as well as solar heat gain
The biggest impact on the energy need for heat is the heat loss due to transmission through the envelope
and du to ventilation. That is true in Denmark because of he relative cold climate and therefore even
more evident in Greenland because of the even colder exterior temperatures.
Both the heat loss due to transmission through the envelope and the heat loss due to ventilation
is very much dependent on the outside temperature as seen in Equation 5.12 and 5.13 showing the
immediate heat loss due to transmission through the building envelope and the due to ventilation
respectively.
The heat loss due to transmission through the building envelope, Φt is calculated by:
Φt = U · A(θi −θe)
(5.12)
where
Φt
is the immediate heat loss due to transmission through the building envelope [W]
U
is the U-value of the building envelope [W/m2K]
A
is the area of the building envelope [m2]
θi
is the interior temperature [°C]
θe
is the exterior temperature [°C]
The heat loss due to transmission through the envelope does also depend on the linear thermal trans-
mission as well as eventually point losses in thermal bridges, as explained in Section 5.2.
The heat loss due to ventilation, Φv is calculated by:
Φv = ρ · cp · qv(θi −θe)(1 −ε)
(5.13)
where
Φv
is the immediate heat loss due to ventilation [W]
ρ
is the density of air (1.205 kg/m3 at 20 °C and 1 atm)
cp
is the specific heat capacity of air (1005 J/kgK at 20 °C and 1 atm)
qv
is the air flow rate [m3/sC]
θi
is the interior temperature [°C]
θe
is the exterior temperature [°C]
ε
is the efficiency of the heat recovery unit [-]
Equation 5.13 emphasizes among others how important a heat recovery unit is in ventilation systems
in arctic regions were exterior temperatures are very low. When fresh air supply comes from fresh air
inlets without any heat recovering it is easy to imagine that the heat loss would be quite significant.
==PAGE:99==
5.3 Energy performance
79
Energy consumption requirements
To prevent a high energy consumption, new constructed buildings have to fulfill a requirement in regards
to energy performance. The requirements differs from country to country. In Greenland the current
building regulation was published in 2006 [18], and is thus rather old compared to the Danish building
regulation, which is from 2018.
Among others the Greenlandic building regulation has a requirement for a buildings energy performance.
There are a three ways to comply with the regulation. The first is to fulfill requirements for the U-value
of specific building elements.
Secondly, the U-values can be changed as long as the total heat loss does not increase compared to the
initial requirements of the U-values. However, the U-values still needs to comply with a second less
strict sets of requirements.
The last is a limit of the buildings energy consumption (the U-values still need to comply with the
minimum requirements). Because of the great climate differences in Greenland, the requirements of the
energy consumption is split into two zones depending on whether the location of the building is below
or above the polar circle. Since Sisimiut is just above the polar circle, the energy consumption limit is
given by:
510 + 325
e
MJ/m2 per year
(5.14)
Where e is the number of floors.
The blocks in Paamaap Kuua are generally 4 stories, and assuming this the yearly energy consumption
limit will be 591 MJ/m2 that is equal to 164 kWh/m2.
The energy consumption limit in the Danish building regulation of 2018 is much more strict, which
among others is due to the warmer climate, and the fact that the Greenlandic building regulation is
from 2006. The energy consumption limit according to the Danish building regulation of 2018 is [8]:
30.0 + 1000
A
kWh/m2 per year
(5.15)
Where A is the heated floor area [m2].
If considering Block 8 of 1668 m2 the yearly energy consumption limit will be 30.6 kWh/m2.
5.3.2
Prerequisites and basis of the analysis
In the following prerequisites and key elements of the basis of the analysis will be described. First the
general aspects of the simulation software is presented, and later a description of how the models are
created will follow.
==PAGE:100==
80
5 Energy study
IDA Indoor Climate and Energy
The following results has been based on the simulation software IDA Indoor Climate and Energy (IDA
ICE) developed by the company EQUA. IDA ICE is highly recognized in the engineering industry and
is generally know as a reliable software when calculating energy performance of buildings. Furthermore
IDA ICE has a number of validations and certifications issued by internal institutions such as ASHRAE,
CEN, LEED, BREAM and DGNB [19].
In the analysis the software version, IDA ICE 4.8 is used. The software is capable of doing a lot of
detailed analysis on a building including calculations on comfort criteria and daylight analysis among
others. In this analysis however, the main focus is to analyse the yearly energy performance of the
scenarios and get an overview of which initiatives that contributes to a decreasing energy consumption
of the apartment blocks. The idea is to see the tendencies rather, than trying too model a perfect
representation of the reality. That said the models have been created to get a good estimation of how
the different initiative influences the energy performances of the building.
Zones
In IDA ICE the building that is analyzed is divided into zones. The zones is usually defined by the
rooms. For each zone in the building model, the user sets up a set of input parameters, such as the
U-value of the various building elements, ventilation air flow into the zone, number of occupants in
the zone etc.
A set of equations is assigned to every zone and based on the input parameters the
performance of the building is simulated (in this case for a whole year).
This methodology means that values of for instance temperatures and air flows is considered to be
homogeneous within each zone. This is can be misleading in some situations if the zone is very large
and for instance the solar heat gain is not evenly distributed in the zone. But in this case were the
overall energy performance of the building is analyzed and the exact temperature distribution within a
zone is not important, this assumption is acceptable.
Weather file
One of the very important input parameters is the weather file. The weather file used in these simulations
consists of data for a test reference year of Sisimiut. In short, a test reference year is constructed by
analyzing weather data from previous years (at least ten years), and then picking the months from the
analyzed period that is most typical to construct a new set of weather data, that represents a typical
year. The weather file provided in this thesis consists of hourly values of dry bulb temperature [°C],
relative humidity [%RF], wind speed [m/s] and direct and diffuse radiation [W/m2]. The weather file
does not include measured data of the wind direction. Instead of a varying wind direction, the wind
will constantly come from the north. This will of course influence the correctness of the analysis and
will be discussed later.
The weather file is provided by assistant professor at DTU Martin Kotol, and can be received by
contacting the authors of this thesis.
==PAGE:101==
5.3 Energy performance
81
Model creation
The building models were first created in the modeling software Autodesk Revit, since this software
has much stronger modeling tools than IDA ICE. Based on an exported IFC-file from Revit, that is
imported into IDA ICE, the geometry of the building and the zones are created in IDA ICE. The
building models are based on original GTO-drawings from Block 8. The Revit model is seen on Figure
5.20 and the analyzed IDA ICE model is seen on Figure 5.21. For the sake of simplification and saving
simulation time, only a part of the building is analyzed in IDA ICE, as seen on the figures. The aim has
been to find a part of the building that is as representative as possible. On Figure 5.22 the analyzed
area is seen on a floor plan of Block 8. Three typical apartments with different sizes are a part of the
analyzed area, where apartments located near the gable and stairwell are included.
As mentioned earlier, the length of the apartment blocks of Paamaap Kuua vary, where Block 8
is longer than the other blocks. Therefore, not the full length of half the block is simulated, as this
effects the impact of the gable on the overall energy performance. And as explained earlier the aim
of the analyses is to see tendencies of the apartment blocks rather than to calculate the exact energy
consumption of a specific block.
The gable of the analyzed area is facing towards north and thus no external facades is facing towards
south. That combined with the fact that the wind direction in the weather file is constantly coming
from the north, leads to the analyzed scenarios is expected to be the most conservative ones. In some
of the scenarios the building is rotated 90 °and 180 °to see impact of the orientation.
As seen on Figure 5.21, the roof has been simplified some, since it is less complex to model a flat
roof. This is of course less correct, but since the roof is a cold ventilated roof where the insulation is
placed on the slab of the roof, this assumption is expected to have little significance.
Figure 5.20: Revit model of the existing Block 8
Figure 5.21: The analyzed IDA ICE model of a
part of the existing Block 8
==PAGE:102==
82

Figure 5.22: Revit floor plan of the existing Block 8 (level 1-3) with the analyzed area to the right

5.3.3
Input parameters
The outcome of the IDA ICE analysis is dependent on how the input parameters are defined. In this
section all input parameters for each scenario is described and discussed. Some input parameters are
defined by zones in which they apply while others apply on a more general level. A lot of the values
presented in this section is based on calculations, that are described in detail in Appendix B.
Building defaults
The characteristics of the building envelope is defined on a building level with some exceptions. The
material layers and properties is defined according to the descriptions in Sections 4.1.5 and 5.1.1.
Table 5.7 lists the U-value for all building elements modelled in IDA ICE. The calculations behind
these values for the existing and proposed building envelope can be found in Appendix B.3 and B.4.
The U-value for the two windows used for modelling is listed in Table 5.8.

5 Energy study
Infiltration
The infiltration is another important parameter, especially in this case, since the facade of the existing
apartment blocks aren’t air tight. Because of the arctic climate, this will influence the energy con-
sumption significantly. For this analysis the air exchange rate at 50 Pa (n50) is set to 2.74 h−1 for
the existing situation. This value are based on the infiltration measured during a blower door test
conducted by Juan Abdulla and Eleni Pavlidi in relation with their master thesis on ”Optimization of
the energy performance of residential blocks in Sisimiut with focus on renovating the building envelope
and installation systems” [40]. This value is used for the existing case as well as for the case where the
U-value of the envelope is improved, but a new ventilation system has not been added. Thus it will be
possible to see the effects of only improving the facade, without improving the ventilation system and
the air tightness. However a set of scenarios is analyzed, where both the U-value and the air tightness
of the facade is improved and a new ventilation system has been implemented. For this case and the
final proposals the infiltration rate at 50 Pa (q50) is 1 l/sperm2 equal to 1.4 h−1. According to the
Danish building regulation of 2018 the infiltration rate at 50 Pa cannot exceed 1 l/sperm2, if a blower
door test has been conducted, and thus this value has been used [8].
Table 5.10: Infiltration rates for the analyzed scenarios
Existing

n50

1.40
Ventilation
The existing apartment blocks have no ventilation systems with supply air. Fresh air is supplied to the
zones by the fresh air inlets in the facade as well as through the less air tight envelope. The supply air
is thus given by the infiltration. The exhaustion is located in kitchen and bathroom. No exhaust units
were found in the small sculleries. The actual exhaust air flow rate is however difficult to estimate since
a lot of factors influence it, such as intentional blockage of openings by occupants. Since the exhaust
air flow rate has great impact in the amount of infiltration though the envelope, different exhaust air
flow rates is analyzed for the existing case. This will be elaborated when the results are presented.
It is assumed that the exhaustion rate is constant and 100 % exhaust rate refers to the requirement
related to exhaust (20 l/s in kitchen and 15 l/s in bathroom) which is identical in the Greenlandic and
Danish building regulation. The existing case is simulated with 100%, 60% and 20% of the current
requirement on exhaustion.
For the scenarios with a new ventilation system, the supply air flow rate is 0.36 l/sperm2 that is
equal to an air change rate of 0.5 h−1, which is the requirement for the apartment as a whole according
to the Greenlandic building regulation. The exhaust air flow rates is balanced to the supply air. Detailed
calculations of the supply and exhaust air flows for specific rooms can be seen in Appendix B.9.
==PAGE:105==
5.3 Energy performance
85
The supply air temperature is set to a default constant value of 16 °C (however as no cooling coil
is added, warmer supply air temperatures might occur in some periods). This value is well below the
heating set point of 20 °C, and thus it is avoided that energy is used to heat the supply air to an
unnecessary degree when the room temperature can be kept at 20 °C by solar and internal heat gains
only. The supply air temperature has in this thesis not been investigated in further details, and issues
such as draught etc. has not been included. The efficiency of the heat recovery unit in the ventilation
system is set to 0.9. The ventilation system has not been investigated in detail in this thesis and the
actual pressure losses has not been calculated (in Section 6.3 the principle of the ventilation ducting
will be explained). Thus the Specific Fan Power (SFP) is set to an default value of 1000 J/m3, which
complies with the maximum allowed value according to the Greenlandic building regulation (2.500
J/m3) and the current Danish building regulation for a ventilation system only supplying one dwelling
(1000 J/m3).

Even though the analyzed apartment block is located in an cold arctic area, the solar heat gain is
not insignificant, especially during the summer when the sun is high. Thus the indoor air temperature
will exceed the limit of 26 °C [14] in some of the analyzed cases, when the solar heat gain is high. Thus,
to prevent this, the windows has been modeled to open when the indoor air temperature exceed 26 °C.
This opening control may not reflect the reality, but it ensures that the indoor air temperatures in the
models are not increasing to an unrealistic level.
Internal heat gains
The internal heat gains relates to the heat from occupants as well as heat from light and equipment,
that is emitted to the zones. To avoid a complex modelling of the internal heat gains, that in reality
depends on a lot of factors, the internal heat gains is set to an constant average value that applies for
all heated areas. The internal heat gains is set to 1.5 W per m2 heated floor area for occupants and 3.5
W per m2 heated floor area for equipment and lighting [1]. To simplify the model in IDA ICE, a total
value of 5 W per m2 heated floor area is given to the parameter Light_equipment_custom.
The small apartments does however have a higher internal gain per m2 heated floor area, since a
minimum heat gain of 210 W per apartment is assumed [1]. Thus the smallest apartments has a total
of internal gains of 6.08 W per m2 heated floor area.
==PAGE:106==
86
5 Energy study
Heating units
The existing apartment blocks have water based radiators in the rooms that are connected to the facade,
and are placed underneath the windows. The same system has been implemented in the IDA ICE models.
Each zone towards the facade has a water based radiator underneath the windows. The heating power
of the radiators are based on the heating needed in the worst case. Both the envelope transmission
and the infiltration, which contributes a lot to the heating demand (especially in the existing case)
is accounted for in the worst case. The heating power of the radiators in the big rooms are 2500 W,
whereas it is 1000 W in the small entrances. The radiator system is kept for all of the analyzed cases
even though in reality the size of the radiators could be decreased in the energy optimized scenarios. In
the zone, where the bathroom and hallway has been merged, an so called ideal heater has been added.
This has been done since the actual heating system in this imaginary zone has not been designed, and
an simplified ideal heater has been added instead, so that the heating demand in the internal zones is
also accounted for in the total energy consumption.
All heating unit has a setpoint of 20 °C, which is the required minimum temperature according to
DS 474 [14].
Domestic hot water
The domestic hot water use is set to 250 litres per m2 heated floor area, which is based on an average
value from SBi 213 [1].
Primary energy factors
The primary energy factor accounts for the actual energy used when taken the production of the different
energy carriers into account. In Denmark the primary energy factor for district heating is 0.85 while
the primary energy factor for electricity is 1.9 [7].
The current Greenlandic building regulations does however not include primary energy factors, and
a overview of how the energy carriers could be weighted in Greenland is not known. Thus the Danish
primary energy factors has been used in the following analysis. This might not be accurate, since the
primary energy factors would probably not be the same in Denmark and Greenland.
Comparing the energy prices for the two countries, could however give an insight in how the different
energy carriers weighs, even though this cannot give the full picture.
According to the national Greenlandic energy supplier, Nukissiorfiit, the price for electricity is 1600
kr/MWh while it is 740 kr/MWh for heat [39].
In Denmark the average price for electricity is 2333 kr/MWh [25], whereas the average price for
heating is 651 kr/MWh for a standard 75 m2 apartment with a yearly district heat consumption of 15
MWh [26].
For both countries the energy prices are within the same ranges, even though the electricity in
Greenland is somewhat cheaper than in Denmark. However it supports the assumption of using the
Danish primary energy factors, when nothing else is known about Greenlandic primary energy factors,
even though it might not be accurate. On the other hand the energy need for heat contributes by far
==PAGE:107==
5.3 Energy performance
87
the most to the total energy consumption, and the energy prices for exactly heat is quite similar in
Denmark and Greenland.
Indeed to fully support the assumption of using Danish primary energy factors in Greenland, a
further investigation of the Greenlandic energy carriers needs to be made.
5.3.4
Energy performance results
In this section the results of the analyses will be presented. All of the analysis should not necessarily be
seen as final renovation proposals. Some of the renovation scenarios are presented to show the design
process and to give insight in which initiatives that are the most effective.
The results of the analyses is divided into five smaller sections:
1. The first analysis investigates the current energy performance of the blocks and gives an estimate
of the yearly energy consumption.
2. The next analysis shows the impact of a new facade with varying insulation thickness. In addition
the impact of a new ventilation system is investigated. In these scenarios the position of the
building envelope is identical to the existing case.
3. Two final renovation proposals is presented in respect to their energy performances. The envelope
of the building has been changed compared to the existing case.
4. All of the analyses will be summarized and presented in a table to get an overview
5. The existing scenario and one of the proposals will be analyzed using a danish weather file, making
it possible to compare it to the danish building regulation
For each scenario the total yearly energy consumption is presented. Furthermore for some analyses the
energy performance for a specific room is presented (this is done as IDA ICE gives detailed results on a
zone level such as the energy balance, but not on a building level). This gives an insight in what leads
to the energy performance of the whole analyzed building. The analyzed room is a standard bedroom
located on the second level in the middle of the building, meaning that the zone has only one external
wall. The room is named 2.5 bedroom in the IDA ICE models, and is thus named hereafter in the
following.
The intention is that the first three sections should give the reader a more detailed insight in the
energy performance and detailed results on a zoning level is thus shown. In contrast, the fourth section
is meant to give an overview of the energy consumption of each scenario. The fifth scenario serves as
an extra analysis.
==PAGE:108==
88
5 Energy study
Existing building
In the following the results of the existing case is being presented. On Figure 5.24 and 5.25 the monthly
energy balance is seen for two variations of the existing case. It is seen that during heating, the heat
loss is coming from the envelope, windows and infiltration. On Figure 5.25 during the coldest months
the heat loss from windows contributes the most. During the summer months the total heat loss of the
windows is less due to solar radiation, where the contribution is actually positive in some month. The
infiltration loss is however very different on the two figures.
As explained the building envelope in the facade is not airtight, and when taking the fresh air inlet
in the facade into account, a big heat loss due to infiltration is expected. Since there is no supply
air from a ventilation system, but only exhaustion in kitchens and bathrooms/toilets in the existing
buildings, the exhaust air flow rate is a crucial parameter when determining the infiltration through
the facade. The higher the exhaustion the more fresh air is entering the apartments. Because of the
very low temperatures especially during the winter, this leads to a big heat loss.
Figure 5.24: Monthly energy balance for the analyzed room (2.5 bedroom) for the existing case with 100% of
the current exhaustion requirement
==PAGE:109==
5.3 Energy performance
89
Figure 5.25: Monthly energy balance for the analyzed room (2.5 bedroom) for the existing case with 20% of
current exhaustion requirement
On Figure 5.26 the envelope transmission is seen for every month for the analyzed room.
It is
seen that the old windows contribute a lot to the envelope transmission. Furthermore it is seen that
even though the thermal bridges contributes some to the envelope transmission, the share is not that
significant since the energy performance of the envelope is generally bad.
Figure 5.26: Monthly envelope transmission for the analyzed room (2.5 bedroom) in the existing case
As seen on Figure 5.25 and 5.24, the different infiltration loss results in quite big heat demand
differences. The same is seen on the total yearly energy consumption for three scenarios with 100%,
60% and 20% of the current exhaustion requirement, in Table 5.12. All of the simulated scenarios is
exceeding the current requirement which is 164 kWh/m2 for this building. For every reduction in the
exhaust rate the yearly energy consumption drops approximately 55 kWh/m2, which is quite significant.
It is highly unlikely that the exhaustion rate is running at a constant maximum. The required
exhaust rate in the current building regulations is only supposed to be running when the kitchen/bath-
room is in use. On the other hand 20 % exhaustion rate might be too little. The reference for the
==PAGE:110==
90
5 Energy study
existing case will be the case with an exhaustion rate of 60 %. This is the most conservative of the
results, if the case of a constant exhaust rate of 100 % is assumed too unlikely.

In Table 5.12 the impact on the orientation is seen for the case with 60% exhaustion. The orien-
tation does not impact a lot. However, when rotating the building 90 °a minor increase in the energy
consumption increases is seen, due to the fact, that a lot of the rooms is facing towards north, where
the wind is blowing from. It was seen that increased infiltration from the north facing rooms outweighs
the decreased infiltration from the south facing rooms. Additionally the solar radiation on the facades
have been changed due to the rotation. When the building is rotated 180 °the energy consumption
decreases since the gable is facing towards the south leading to higher solar radiation and less exposure
to the wind, resulting in less heat demand.
==PAGE:111==
5.3 Energy performance
91
New facade
In this part, an improved facade system will be investigated, as explained in Section 5.1. As the facade is
non bearing, the whole facade is replaced by a new one, with improved windows and wall system. In the
following three wall systems is investigated with 200mm, 300mm and 400mm insulation. These three
analyses will be positioned at the same place as the existing facade and will be given the same infiltration
rate. This is done to see the effect of the improved value of the new envelope only. Furthermore it
seems reasonable to implement fresh air inlets in the new facade if, a ventilation system with supply
air is not added, and as a result the existing infiltration rate might be suitable. The exhaustion rate of
these three scenarios corresponds to 60 % of maximum exhaustion.
The same three wall systems will also be analyzed with a new ventilation system with both supply
and exhaustion. Thus the infiltration rate of the new facade is improved since the fresh air inlets are
no linger needed. In total six analysis is presented:
• New facade:
– 200 mm insulation
– 300 mm insulation
– 400 mm insulation
• New facade with improved air tightness and new ventilation system:
– 200 mm insulation
– 300 mm insulation
– 400 mm insulation
Figure 5.27: Monthly energy balance for the analyzed room (2.5 bedroom) with new facade 200mm
==PAGE:112==
92
5 Energy study
Figure 5.28: Monthly energy balance for the analyzed room (2.5 bedroom) with new facade 200mm and new
ventilation system
On Figure 5.27 and 5.28 the difference in the monthly energy balance is seen for the case with a new
facade with 200 mm insulation. It is clearly seen that the case with a new ventilation system and no
fresh air inlets, have much less infiltration. On Figure 5.28 it is instead seen that the supply air from
the ventilation system influences the energy balance. The temperature is set to a default minimum
value of 16 °C as mentioned, and has thus a bigger negative impact in the summer month were the
room temperature is higher. Furthermore it is seen that during the warmest month the infiltration is
rather high. This is due to opening of windows during periods were the room temperature is exceeding
26 °C, as explained. In these month the solar heat gain is quite significant, since the sun is present
most of the day during these month in Greenland.
The figure does not show the energy needed to heat the supply air. For the case with a ventilation
system the total yearly zone heating and AHU heating is 31 MWh combined, where the AHU heating is
responsible for approximately 32 % of that. The so called utilized free heat from the AHU heat recovery
is in this case 28 MWh for a year. In comparison to the case without a ventilation system the total
yearly zone heating is 59 MWh, which is almost the double compared to the combined zone heating
and AHU heating in the case with a ventilation system. This shows the importance of a ventilation
system with heat exchanger in cold climates.

Figure 5.29: Envelope transmission of the ana-
lyzed room (2.5 bedroom) in March
Figure 5.30:
Envelope transmission of a room
located by the gable (2.7 bedroom) in March
On Figure 5.29 the envelope transmission for the coldest month, March, is seen for the analyzed
room with the three different wall systems. Compared to the analyzed room in the existing case (with
a total envelope transmission for March of approximately -228 kWh), all new wall systems has been
increased by more than half. When the transmission through the walls and especially the windows
has been decreased the thermal bridges are becoming more evident, as seen in Section 5.2.4 - Heat
flow analysis. No surprisingly it is seen that the transmission through the walls (as well as the total
transmission) is decreasing less when going from a 300 mm insulation thickness to a 400 mm insulation
thickness compared to going from 200 mm to 300 mm.
On Figure 5.30 the envelope transmission is seen for a similar room that is located by the gable.
Because of the position the transmission through the walls is heavily increased. The amount of windows
is however identical to 2.5 bedroom. The most interesting thing is however that the thermal bridges
contributes much more to the envelope transmission because of the thermal bridge in the gable wings.
An eventual elimination of the thermal bridges will in this case have much bigger impact on the energy
consumption.

In Table 5.13 the total yearly energy consumption for six analyzed cases is seen. All of the new
scenarios with improved facade is within the requirement of 164 kWh/m2. However it is seen that
adding a new ventilation system with heat exchanger instead of just having fresh air inlets in the
facade improves the energy consumption significantly. For every wall system the energy consumption
is reduced by approximately 38 %. Furthermore for the three scenarios with new ventilation system,
going from the wall system with 200 mm insulation to the wall system with 300 mm insulation decreases
the energy consumption by 6.7 kWh/m2whereas it is further reduced by 3.8 kWh/m2 when improving
the insulation thickness to 400 mm. As expected, improving the insulation thickness from 300 mm to
400mm is not as efficient as going from 200 mm insulation to 300 mm insulation. Instead there is a
bigger potential in eliminating the thermal bridges. Taking into account the extra environmental and
economical cost of shipping building materials to Greenland a 400 mm insulated facade might not be
reasonable. A further investigation on the performance of insulation thickness versus the environmental
and economical cost related to shipping is necessary to assess the exact consequences of different wall
systems. This is not included in this thesis. However the next sets of analysis has a wall system with
300 mm insulation thickness.

Renovation proposals
For the final two renovation proposals, the existing location of the building envelope has been moved,
which has partly been done to eliminate the thermal bridges while preserving the load bearing concrete
structures (see Section 5.1 for description). Furthermore a new ventilation system has been added. The
yearly energy consumption of the two proposals is seen in Table 5.14. The elimination of the thermal
bridges has lead to another significantly reduced energy consumption, and is way beneath the required
limit of 164 kWh/m2. Compared to the scenario with a new wall system with 300mm insulation that
had an yearly energy consumption of 70.8 kWh/m2, the two proposals has further reduced the yearly
energy consumption by 17.6 kWh/m2 and 12.2 kWh/m2 respectively.
As for the initial analysis, the building in second proposal has been rotated 90 °and 180°. Again this
is done to see how much the orientation impacts the results. As seen earlier the orientation influences
the energy consumption some, but not significantly.

5.3 Energy performance

Energy performance in Denmark
In this section two extra analyses are shown. The existing case with 60% exhaustion and Proposal 2
with 300 mm insulation are simulated using a danish climate file (Danish Design Reference Year, DRY,
created in 2013 [1]). In Table 5.16 the results are shown.
The existing case has a total yearly energy consumption of 116.2 kWh/m2, which is equal to an
energy category D, if Block 8 is considered [45].
According to statistics of apartment blocks built
between 1961 to 1972 in Denmark, a category D is also the most typical energy performance.
The new energy optimized proposal will have an energy consumption of 29.2 kWh/m2, which is
just within the requirement of the Danish building regulation of 2018. For Block 8 the requirement is
30.6 kWh/m2.
The original results using a weather file from Sisimiut showed an yearly energy consumption of 243.0
kWh/m2 and 58.6 kWh/m2 for the existing case with 60% exhaustion and proposal 2 respectively,
showing a significant difference.

5.4
Moisture issues resulting from low surface temperatures around
thermal bridges
Moisture is considered as the most frequent cause of damages in our buildings [5]. Long term exposure to
moisture can cause deformation of building components, mould growth and material degradation due to
rot, salt and frost erosion. Moisture related issues are important to consider when renovating buildings,
as an energy renovation of the facade will change the thermal properties of the building. Additionally,
the moisture content indoors may vary due to change in building use, moisture production, air exchange
rate etc. The risk of condensation may be reduced by re-insulating the building. However, there is a risk
that the mentioned parameters can create conditions more prone to moisture than before the renovation.
Since the existing facade is not re-insulated, but replaced with a new, it is assumed that the moisture
conditions in the facade system will be ensured sufficiently if recommended initiatives are implemented
(this will be further explained in Section 6.4 - Buildability). As a result, no additional investigations
on moisture in the new facade construction is made in this thesis.
Moisture issues resulting from
the existing thermal bridges might however still be present after a renovation and thus this has been
examined.
Thermal bridges are a critical area regarding condensation, as the surface temperature is usually
lower than the adjacent surface temperatures. According to SBi 221, the risk of condensation is small
if the surface temperature at the thermal bridge can be calculated to be minimum 16.5◦C with indoor
and outdoor boundary conditions at 20◦C and 0◦C [37].
However, this assumption applies to the
Danish climate and moisture conditions, and may not necessarily apply for the Greenlandic conditions.
Therefore, a more extensive analysis of the moisture conditions must be conducted for this renovation
case. This has been done using the same method as mentioned in Section 5.2 for determining internal
surface temperatures along thermal bridges using COMSOL. In the following the thermal bridges in
the facade is assessed, which are at the cantilevers and the gable wings. The thermal bridge in the
foundation is not investigated in the following, since it is assumed that the surface temperatures will
not drop to a point where condensation and mold growth will pose a risk. This is mainly due to the
fact that this thermal bridge is not directly exposed to the outdoors, but to the ground floor where
the temperatures are expected to be higher than outside. Furthermore, earlier analyses shows that
the minimum indoor surface temperature are generally higher in this thermal bridge compared to the
others.
Beside the analysis on moisture issues, this section includes an additional analysis of how the low
surface temperatures in thermal bridges might influence comfort related to down draught.
5.4.1
General considerations on moisture in buildings
As any other given gas, air can contain moisture [5]. However, air can only hold a certain amount of
moisture, where warm air can contain more moisture than cold air. The maximum amount of moisture
==PAGE:119==
5.4 Moisture issues resulting from low surface temperatures around thermal bridges
99
air can maintain at a given temperature is called the dew point. The graph in figure 5.31 shows the
saturation point for temperatures -10◦C to 30◦C. As seen on the graph, when the temperature increases,
the air can contain more moisture and the dew point increases. When the temperature declines, as does
the dew point.
Water content in air [g/m3]
Temperature [°C]
Figure 5.31: Water vapour diagram [5]
When considering two cases with air at different temperatures, the hot air may contain more moisture
than cold air. However, the colder air may be closer to being saturated. Therefore, relative humidity
(RH) is used to describe the relation between the actual moisture content and maximum water content
in air. Air at lower temperatures may have a low absolute amount of water, but have a high relative
humidity. Relative humidity is given by:
RH = v
vs
∗100%
(5.16)
where
v
actual water content
vs
maximum water content possible (saturated)
When the relative humidity reaches 100%, condensation will arise. However, condensation is not the
only concern regarding elevated relative humidity indoors. Mould growth is also important to consider.
There are several factors on which mould is dependant on to thrive. These growth conditions include
relative humidity, nutrients, temperature and time.
All parameters must together match the right
growth conditions for mould to arise. According to SBI 224, the critical limit for humidity is 75% RH
regarding mould growth, and if the relative humidity is kept below this limit, the risk of mould growth
is minimal.
==PAGE:120==
100
5 Energy study
The indoor relative humidity is dependent on the temperature and indoor moisture content. The
moisture content indoors is a combination of: moisture content outdoors, moisture production indoors,
ventilation and building volume. The moisture content is given by the equation:
vi = ve + G
nV
(
1 −e−nt)
(5.17)
where
vi
is the water content indoors [kg/m3]
ve
is the water content outdoors [kg/m3]
G
is the moisture production [kg/h]
n
is the air change rate, given in n = L
V [h−1]
L
is the amount of supplied fresh air [m3/h]
V
is the building volume [m3]
t
is time [h]
It is the internal moisture conditions after a while (for large values of t) which are of interest. Thus,
the following reduction of the equation is reasonable:
vi = ve + G
nV
(5.18)
From equation 5.18 it is seen that the indoor moisture content is influenced by two terms, where ve is the
moisture contribution from outdoor moisture content and the last term describes the internal moisture
contribution. The outdoor moisture contribution is generally lower in Greenland than Denmark. Due
to the low outdoor temperatures in Greenland, the absolute amount of water content supplied from the
outdoor water content is low. The internal moisture contribution is dependent on the air change rate
and the moisture production, which varies from household to household. There are several moisture
producing activities which are a part of the daily routine. Moisture is submitted to the room just
by having people present (through breath and sweat), when washing dishes, drying clothes, bathing,
cleaning, etc. A danish study on moisture production in an home with two children and two adults,
found a daily average moisture production to be approximately 8.5 kg [34]. Table 5.17 lists the moisture
contribution distributed between sources. Furthermore it is seen that the air change rate effects

5.4.2
Calculations on moisture issues related to thermal bridges
When warm indoor air is cooled down by a lower surface temperature along a thermal bridge, the relative
humidity of the air will rise. If the relative humidity rises to 100% condensation will occur. Additionally,
if the relative humidity rises above 75% the risk of mould growth is more critical. Therefore, an analysis
of the temperatures of the thermal bridges in the different renovation strategies are assessed. The risk of
moisture and mould development is assessed for the thermal bridge along the gable wing and cantilever.
The outdoor temperature and relative humidity fluctuates from month to month. As the outdoor
relative humidity effects the moisture contribution to the indoor water content, and the outside temper-
ature effects the surface temperature of the thermal bridge, each month must be assessed for evaluating
the ”worst case moisture scenario”.
The monthly mean temperature (θe) and monthly mean relative humidity (RH) for Sisimiut, is used
to find the monthly moisture contribution (ve) to the indoor water content. This is calculated by using
linear interpolation on table values from SBI 224 and Equation 5.16, where v=ve and vs=interpolated
value. Table can be found in Appendix B.10, Table B.5.
For this analysis the air exchange rate (n) is set to 0.22 h−1 for the existing situation and 0.5 h−1 for
the proposal scenarios. For the existing scenario the precise air exchange rate is unknown. It is assumed
that the main air exchange is by infiltration through the building envelope as mentioned in Section 5.3
Energy Performance. The infiltration is dependent on variable factors (wind speed and exhaust). As
the exact influence of these factors are not not known, the the normalized infiltration rate (SBi 213 [1])
based on the aforementioned blower door test, is used in this assessment (see Appendix B.8). This value
might be too conservative compared to reality, but as the existing air exchange is based on infiltration,
there is also a risk that the air exchange will be this low for some time periods.
The Greenlandic building regulations requires an air exchange rate of 0.5 h−1 [18]. Thus, this is
used for the renovation scenario.
==PAGE:122==
102
5 Energy study
The moisture conditions is considered for a family of four. For this, an a three bedroom apartment
of 69.3 m2 is considered. With a room height of 2.68 m, the calculations are based on a volume of 185.7
m3 for all scenarios.
Using COMSOL Multiphysics, the minimum indoor point temperature (θminExist.) has been calcu-
lated for a model representing the thermal bridges. The cantilever is modelled as a 3D geometrical model
with quasi-homogeneous layers. Whilst the gable wing is modelled two dimensional. The boundary con-
ditions used for the modelling are an indoor air temperature of 20◦C and for the exterior conditions
the mean air temperature for each month is used.
Figure 5.32: Minimum temperature in August for existing construction from COMSOL Multiphysics
Equation 5.18 can be used to find the indoor moisture content. However, assumptions on uncertain
parameters may have a large influence on the results.
In this case, the moisture production G is
unknown for an average Greenlandic family. Thus, the moisture production is evaluated instead. The
maximum amount of moisture production (before the relative humidity reaches 100% and 75%) is found
for the minimum indoor surface temperature by the thermal bridges. Firstly, the moisture production
for reaching a relative humidity of 100% is evaluated for determining the worst case scenario. The water
content when saturated (vi,sat.) at the point temperature was found using linear interpolation on the
values from Table B.5 found in Appendix B.10. The water content for relative humidity of 75% is found
using Diagram B.4 also found in Appendix B.10.
Table 5.18 summarizes the monthly parameters for determining the moisture production before
condensation arises at the thermal bridge. The month of August is seen as the most critical month
when regarding moisture. July is seen to have a greater outdoor moisture contribution, but the colder
temperature in August, causes condensation at less moisture production.
However, according to SBI 224 the moisture contribution decreases when the temperature rises. The
reduction is a response to increased air exchange due to increase of natural ventilation (through opening
==PAGE:123==
5.4 Moisture issues resulting from low surface temperatures around thermal bridges
103
of windows). Temperatures below 0◦C has a constant moisture contribution, and temperatures above
have a decreasing contribution. The graph describing these tendencies can be found in Appendix B.10,
Figure B.5. The temperatures in Greenland during summer does not reach 20◦C, where according to
SBI 224 the moisture contribution indoors can be neglected. However, they still reach a temperature
(above 0◦C) where the possibility of using natural ventilation is present according to the graph. It is
therefore likely that the worst month is one of the month were the the monthly mean temperature is
below 0◦C, since natural ventilation is unlikely. Thus March may be the worst month, since a low limit
for maximum moisture is present and it is less likely that natural ventilation will occur. However, it
is seen that the limit values do not vary substantially from month to month. Additionally, the exact
impact of natural ventilation in a Greenlandic setting is not known. Therefore, it is reasonable to use
the results from August to give an indication of the moisture conditions in the worst case.
