Comparing the cantilever and gable wing, it is seen that the cantilever is in most cases slightly
more critical regarding moisture production, except in the 400mm scenario, where the gable is slightly
worse. It is also seen that the renovation proposals performs (with existing ventilation rate) slightly
better than the existing scenario, and that increasing insulation thickness in general results in a higher
maximum moisture production before risk of condensation and mold growth is reached. However, when
a new ventilation with a higher ventilation is implemented it is seen that the maximum allowed moisture
production is increased significantly.
In the analysis of the existing construction it is seen that the maximum moisture production is 6.78
kg/day before the limit for condensation is reached and as low as 3.43 kg/day before reaching 75%
humidity. The analysis shows that for very low levels of moisture contribution, there is a risk of good
growth conditions for mould and condensation. The analysis may have used an air exchange rate too
conservative compared to reality. However, as the air exchange is based on infiltration, there is also a
risk that in time periods the air exchange will be this low in reality. Furthermore, intentional blockage
of openings will influences the air change rate negatively. When a new facade is implemented but the
existing ventilation rate is kept the added insulation results in a higher indoor surface temperature and
as a results, the moisture production limit is increased. Still the limit for risk of condensation as well
as mould growth is within or very close to the aforementioned average moisture production for a family
on 8.5 kg/day.
For the three renovation proposals with a new ventilation system it is seen that as long as the
==PAGE:125==
5.4 Moisture issues resulting from low surface temperatures around thermal bridges
105
moisture production is under 17.4 kg/day there is no risk of condensation along both assessed thermal
bridges, for all insulation thicknesses. However, when concerning the relative humidity limit of 75%,
the maximum moisture production fluctuates from 9.13 to 11.35 kg/day for the three insulation cases of
the cantilever. The aforementioned study on average daily moisture production for a family stated an
average moisture production on 8.5 kg/day, and if that is the reference the limit for moisture production
is not reached. However, it can be discussed whether an average Greenlandic family may be prone to
have greater moisture production. Drying snow covered winter clothing and boots indoors, contributes
as a new moisture source. Additionally, the social life in the Greenlandic community is spent more
indoors than compared to Denmark, resulting in a greater moisture contribution. Another source of
elevated moisture production is that the traditional cooking in the Greenlandic kitchen is mainly based
on cooking rather than roasting and frying which also generates a lot of moisture. These contributions
may result in a significantly higher moisture contribution compared with the average Danish family.
However, further studies must be conducted to state how much.
This assessment is based on mean monthly values for external temperatures and relative humidity.
Greater temperature fluctuations will result in lower surface temperatures indoors, that will affect the
risk of condensation and mould growth. Additionally, it is shown that the ventilation rate impacts
the risk of condensation and mould growth significantly.
Should the ventilation for any reason be
prevented in working sufficiently, the risk of condensation will be far greater. This assessment is based
on a ventilation system with no moisture recovery, that might be implemented in an otherwise dry
arctic climate. If a ventilation system with moisture recovery is used, the indoor moisture content is
elevated, causing values of higher relative humidity, and the risk of condensation is greater. All these
uncertainties means, that the risk of mould growth can not be dismissed if the thermal bridges are not
eliminated, even though a new facade and new ventilation system is implemented. The fact that it is
not unlikely that the moisture production in an Greenlandic family is higher than a Danish family, adds
to the importance of handling the thermal bridges.
5.4.3
Extra - Minimum surface temperature
Low surface temperatures are not only of interest due to its effect on the local relative humidity, but also
on the thermal comfort or discomfort for people in the room. Thermal discomfort can be to feel too warm
or too cool in a given environment, but may also be caused by unwanted local cooling or heating of the
body [15]. Common discomfort factors include radiant temperature asymmetry, exposure to draught,
vertical air temperature difference, and cold or warm floors. According to SBI 221 Efterisolering af
etageboliger the risk of downdraught due to low surface temperature by the thermal bridge is eliminated
if the minimum surface temperature is calculated to be 16.5◦C with 0◦C outside and 20◦C inside [37].
Whether this temperature difference is based on an assumption of a typical cold winter day with 0
◦C outdoor temperature, is unknown. But as shown in Chapter 3 - Pre study, the average monthly
outdoor temperature during winter in Denmark is around 0◦C. To compare, the lowest monthly mean
temperature in Sisimiut is -17.1 ◦C in March. An analysis of the minimum indoor surface temperatures
for the scenarios with -17.1◦C outside and 20◦C inside is seen in Table 5.20. It is seen that for all
==PAGE:126==
106
5 Energy study
the scenarios the temperatures are below 16.5◦C. This indicates that downdraught due to the low
surface temperature might cause a problem. However, as the exact premises in the analysis from SBi
is not known, this is only an indication, and thus the results shown in Table 5.20 cannot necessarily
be directly compared to that analysis. However, the analysis shows, that comfort issues related to the
thermal bridges can not be ignored.

5.5
Considerations on the energy study
From the presented analyses in this chapter, the energy performance of the existing apartment blocks
as well as the renovation proposals has been investigated.
It has been found that the issues related to the thermal bridges needs to be handled when implement-
ing a new energy optimized facade. It is shown that the thermal bridges contributes more and more to
the total envelope transmission when the insulation thickness of the facade is increased. Additionally,
the analysis has shown that the risk of condensation and mould growth due to low surface temperatures
near thermal bridges cannot be completely neglected when the Greenlandic setting is taken into account.
However, further studies needs to be made on this area. Nevertheless, this adds to the arguments for
handling the thermal bridges.
Furthermore, when implementing a new and more airtight facade, a new ventilation system needs
to be implemented in addition to supply sufficient amount of fresh air. The analysis has additionally
shown that a ventilation system with a heat recovery unit is essential in the arctic.
During the process, two renovation proposals has been designed where the thermal bridges in the
facade (cantilever and gable wing) are eliminated. The functional design of these two proposals will be
investigated in the next chapter, where the principle layout of the ventilation system will be presented
as well.
==PAGE:127==
CHAPTER 6
Concept and design
development
Based on earlier chapters, this chapter investigates and discusses different solutions regarding the ren-
ovation design. Two final renovation proposals - one based on the existing design, and one with a
less conventional design - will be compared regarding to functional design. The energy performance of
the envelope of both proposals have already been investigated in the energy study, and both designs
have shown to improve the energy performance significantly compared to the existing apartment blocks.
After both designs have been investigated and compared, one renovation proposal will be chosen and
this design will be investigated in further detail. The layout of the floor plans and the incorporation
of a new ventilation system will be presented. Additionally, considerations on the buildability will be
presented and lastly the appearance of the new building envelope will be discussed.
6.1
Investigations on the design of the final proposals
In the following the general design of two final renovation proposals will be assessed. As shown in
Section 5.1, the facade of the proposals is extended such that the balcony cantilevers are enclosed in
the new envelope. Every facade element in Proposal 1 is extended, meaning that the building form is
quite similar to the existing case. The access balconies are created in similar style as the existing access
balconies as seen on Figure 6.1. The stairwells are however left outside at the gables, and load bearing
columns have replaced the existing load bearing cantilevers, supporting the balconies. The building
form of Proposal 2 is however different to the existing. Some facade elements are kept at the existing
position, creating a less strict building form, as seen on Figure 6.2. Additionally, a new access system
has been implemented, creating less access balconies.
As explained earlier the first floor has been insulated from underneath, leaving the ground floor as
unheated area. As a result, the few apartments located at ground level are being transformed to space
for storage, freezers, open kitchen to handle fish and game, etc. The lost living area in the ground level
is gained by both extending the facade and including the existing stairwells as living area. Additionally,
this extra space gives opportunities for splitting up the biggest apartments if needed. This is elaborated
in the next section. In Table 6.1 the heated, unheated and total floor area is seen for the existing Block
8 and the two renovation proposals. Even though the living areas on the ground level is removed, both
==PAGE:128==
108
6 Concept and design development
proposals have more heated floor area for living. Additionally, it is seen that there is significantly more
unheated area for storage and other functions. Not surprisingly Proposal 1 has more heated floor area
than Proposal 2.
Another interesting aspect is the potential of adding extra floors on top of the existing building.
Assuming the load bearing concrete structure can withstand extra loads from new floors, this could add
extra variations to the proposals and is a further argument for leaving the ground floor unheated. Since
the construction of a new roof that fits the new building envelope is almost inevitable, it creates an
opportunity to investigate these potentials. An in depth analysis of the existing load bearing structure
and a further investigation of the new structure is needed to assess this. Since this is not a part of this
thesis, the proposals is made with a roof similar to the existing.

6.1.1
Accessibility and privacy
On Figure 6.3 and 6.4 the principles of how the apartments is accessed are seen for the two renovation
proposals.
Proposal 1, which design is quite similar to the existing apartment blocks, has access
balconies that leads the residents from the outdoor stairwells at the gables to the apartments. This
creates a simple access way for the residents, and furthermore these balconies establishes easy access to
the outdoors. Additionally, it creates an environment where it is easy to encounter your neighbours.
==PAGE:129==
6.1 Investigations on the design of the final proposals
109
The principle for accessibility of Proposal 2 is quite different. An extra stairwell has been added,
and placed such that the access balconies are minimized. This is done for a number of reasons. First
of all, the extension of the facade, has a negative impact on the daylight in the parts furthest away
from the windows. By not implementing the conventional access balcony, the daylight is less blocked
and more daylight is led into the apartments. Furthermore it gives a better unblocked view to the
outdoors. Additionally, the conventional access balconies restricts your feeling of privacy. It may be
very unpleasant to see people walking past the kitchen or bedroom window. Furthermore, since the
access balconies are relatively narrow it can be unpleasant for people to walk past windows, leaving
one exposed. It might be convenient to get close to your neighbours, but the distances between the
windows and the access ways in this case are much likely to interfere with ones privacy. Only in the
smallest apartment are the neighbors to the left walking past the kitchen window. Otherwise your are
lead directly to your apartment or walking past your own windows.
Figure 6.3: A floor plan for Proposal 1 showing the principle of accessing the apartments through the access
balconies. The narrow private balconies on the other side of the building is marked
Figure 6.4: A floor plan for Proposal 2 showing the principle of accessing the apartments through the outdoor
stairwells that is located to minimize balcony area. The new and wider (but shorter) private balconies is marked
==PAGE:130==
110
6 Concept and design development
The private balconies in Proposal 2 are changed compared to the conventional ones. Again the
design increases the amount of daylight in many of the rooms, as well as the feeling of privacy. The size
of the balconies are reduced a bit, but the new size is found to be more beneficial than the conventional
one. The conventional balconies are quite narrow whereas the added depth of the new ones creates
a more useful design. Since the balconies of Proposal 2 are shorter and is positioned in a ”pocket”
it is assumed that the load bearing columns (from Proposal 1) can be replaced by a less visible load
carrying system. On Figure 6.5 the principle of the structural system is seen for two types of balconies.
The anchoring points are marked with red. The balcony in Proposal 1 is carried by columns, whereas
the balcony in Proposal 2 is anchored to the facade. Depending on the actual depth of the balcony, a
wire connecting the facade and the outermost position of the balcony in Proposal 2 might be needed as
shown. The actual design of the structural system is not investigated in detail in this thesis. Only the
principle is shown.

6.2
Layout of the floor plans
In this section the design of the floor plans will be described. First the initiatives behind the layout
of the different apartments will be explained. This will be follow by an investigation on the possible
utilization of the unheated ground floor.
6.2.1
Apartment types
The layout of the apartments are based on the original layout, since the design of existing apartments
is generally well made. Additionally the load bearing inner walls constraints the freedom of design to
some extent. Three different apartment types has been created, where the biggest apartment type can
be split into two. The layout of the apartment is seen on Figure 6.6. The shown apartment types
are the basis of the whole apartment block with minor modification. Some apartments are mirrored
and one apartment B has a slightly bigger entrance. Otherwise the layout of the five apartment types
describes all of the apartments.
Kitchen and entrances/sculleries
In the following the terms scullery and entrance is used. In this thesis a scullery refers to an entrance that
in addition to being an entrance, also has a washing machine and/or a drying machine. Furthermore
the size of the scullery allows the residents to dry clothes in the room. The small entrances however
refers just to an entrance, where outdoor clothes and boots can be stored. As shown earlier the facade
of the renovation proposal is extended which creates extra depth in many of the rooms. To avoid too
narrow rooms, the kitchen is also increased in the other direction by including the small entrances, and
new big kitchens with space for eating is created (this is however not done in the smallest apartments,
Apartment C and D1). The small entrances have been replaced by a much bigger scullery, where the
small existing room just next to the kitchen has been included for the purpose. This has been one of the
important initiatives, since the small existing entrances doesn’t fulfill the needs of Greenlandic living
and climate. The size of the sculleries has increased significantly creating space to leave outdoor wear
for drying. Furthermore there is space for a washing machine if needed. The smallest apartments have
a smaller entrance, but the number of occupants in these types of apartments is expected to be fewer
as well.
Bathrooms and storage rooms
The extension of the facade creates areas in the apartments that are not well lit, even though the access
balconies are minimized. Thus in some of the poorly lit places storage rooms has been placed. Many
==PAGE:132==
112
6 Concept and design development
of the narrow rooms has a storage room ind the end furthest away from the windows. And extra non
bearing wall is placed with a simple sliding door.
The bathrooms are kept at the same place as the existing, in the middle of the building. Instead of
having a two individual zones for a toilet and a shower as for the existing case, this has been merged into
one bathroom zone, containing a toilet and a shower. To be able to create the two split apartments, it
is however necessary to create and extra bathroom in Apartment D1. For this purpose a new plumbing
system needs to be made. This has however not been investigated further in this thesis, but a similar
system as the existing is assumed to be implemented. Furthermore an extra kitchen has been created in
Apartment D2. This kitchen has been placed in the end of the room near the other water and plumbing
services. The actual design has not been investigated further.
Door openings
Another important aspect has been to reuse the existing door openings in the load bearing concrete
walls, so that the structural system is not influenced. On Figure 6.6 new door openings as well as
existing door openings that have been sealed is marked on the detailed floor plans. A new door opening
has been added in a load bearing wall in Apartment A, where the stairwell is included as living space.
It is assumed that this wouldn’t interfere with the structural capacities of the load bearing system.
In Apartment D1 and D2 the original design has been changed significantly, but it does not interfere
much with the existing load bearing walls. In apartment D1 a new door opening between the hallway
and the storage has been added in a load bearing wall. Additionally a relatively large opening is created
between the kitchen and the living room. In Apartment D2 no new door opening has been added in
load bearing walls. However the existing door opening in the scullery has been sealed.
In general these minor changes to the structural system is assumed not to influence the overall load
bearing performance. A structural analysis of this needs to be made to verify these assumptions, but
that is not included in this thesis.

Flexible apartments solutions
One of the aims of the renovation proposal has been to create solutions that are flexible, such that
the apartment types can fit different needs. The original proposal consists of nine Apartment A, nine
Apartment B and three Apartment C. In Table 6.2 this is composition is compared to the existing
apartment types. The table lists the number of different apartment types. It is important to keep in
mind, that the apartments and the rooms in general have grown in size, even though the number of
rooms have increased.
In general it is seen that the number of rooms is reduced. That is mainly due the fact that the
kitchen is increased in size and that a bigger entrance has been placed in the adjacent room as explained.
Some of the 4 room apartments has however the same number of rooms since the stairwells has been
included as an extra room. Furthermore it is seen that the total number of apartments decreases a little
because of the new unheated ground level. Even though the total heated floor area has been increased,
it has not been a wish to decrease the number of apartments after the renovation. Since the biggest
apartments have grown significantly in size, a possible solution of how this type of apartment can be
divided into two. Apartment D1 has an entrance similar to the one in the small Apartment C, whereas
a bigger scullery has been implemented in Apartment D2. Both apartments has space for storage as
well as combined kitchen and living room space. Only Apartment D2 has a balcony however. Since
these apartment types is significantly bigger than the small Apartment C, and have a combined kitchen
and living room space, they are referred to as 1½ room apartments.
The renovation proposal is flexible in the sense, that different apartment solutions can be created
without compromising with the design of the envelope. All of the Apartment A types can be divided if
it is wished to create more apartments. The only major thing that has to be changed is, that an extra
kitchen and bathroom needs to be implemented for one of the apartments. In Table 6.2 it is shown
that a total of 30 apartments can be created, substantially more than the originally proposal and more
than the existing case as well. Other solutions where only some of the Apartment A is divided is of
course also possible. In addition the Apartments A is very much suitable for a shared flat as well. And
as mentioned earlier, the potential of adding a extra level when renovating the apartment blocks could
be considering, which will even add to the number of apartments.
The aim of the layouts on Figure 6.6 is not necessarily to show exactly how the layout of the
apartments should be. The idea is to show that the principle behind the renovation creates different
and suitable apartments, that moreover is flexible in the sense that different types of apartments can
be created without interfering with the general design. For instance, if it is desired to create smaller
apartments for couples or singles with a low income, the renovation proposal includes this solution. The
design can be implemented in different places where the needs might be different.

The two technical rooms are placed similar to the original building, as the system for heating and

6.3
Ventilation principles
Ensuring sufficient ventilation is important to consider when renovating the building envelope.
As
the air tightness of the envelope is improved to reduce the heat loss through infiltration, the natural
infiltration is reduced. Sufficient air change is essential for ensuring a good indoor air quality, and for
removal of odor and damp. Implementing a modern ventilation system in an existing building can be a
challenge, as the new system may have different requirements than the existing system. In this section
the essential principles behind implementing a ventilation system will be covered. However, a more
thorough investigation on the ventilation and dimensioning of the system has not been conducted.
==PAGE:136==
116
6 Concept and design development
6.3.1
Ventilation system
Generally, there are two options when choosing a mechanical system for ventilating larger buildings,
centralized or decentralized system.
A decentralized ventilation system is when each apartment is
utilized with its individual unit, whilst a centralized system has one ventilation unit supplying all
apartments. There are several pros and cons for the two systems. For a project with a large quantity of
dwellings a centralized system may be a cheaper expense, when compared to buying several decentralized
units. However, when purchasing several units, it is not a large expense to buy a few extra for replacing
or repairing. Additionally, by having several independent units, the whole system will not be influenced
if single units are not running optimal (either due to malfunction or misuse by occupants). A centralized
system demands an insulated ventilation room for placing the large air handling unit, typically placed
in the attic or basement. By placing the air handling units in the apartments, they are placed in an
already heated area. The existing shaft work for the existing mechanical ventilation is too small for
a new centralized system, and would therefore require expansion of the existing shafts or establishing
new. It is chosen to focus on a solution based on a decentralized system due to the arguments stated
above.
A heat exchanger is necessary for exploiting the heat from the exhausted air in the apartments to
heat the cold fresh air coming into the apartment. This can be done using Heat Recovery Ventilation
(HRV) or Energy Recovery Ventilation (ERV). The main difference between the two systems is that
the HRV only recovers the heat, whilst an ERV recovers both heat and moisture. The apartments will
be ventilated with air with a low relative humidity when the cold arctic air from outside is heated to
the internal conditions. Despite that the internal moisture production may assist on raising the relative
humidity, there may be time periods with low moisture production and constant ventilation, resulting
in dry indoor air and discomfort for the inhabitants. The indoor air quality under these circumstances
would benefit with a ventilation system with moisture recovery and therefore an ERV heat exchanger
may be favourable.
When the outdoor temperatures are below the dew point of the extracted air, condensation will
arise in the heat exchanger. If the outdoor temperature is below 0◦C the moisture will freeze, causing
ice formation in the heat exchanger. Preheating the outdoor air to above 0◦C is a commonly used
anti-freeze strategy for handling this issue. This has however not been investigated further.
6.3.2
Ventilation rate
The Greenlandic building regulations requires an air exchange rate of 0.5 h−1[18]. Additionally there
is a requirement that the following exhaust rates must be met: kitchen 20 l/s, bathroom 15 l/s and
scullery 10 l/s.
In the renovated apartments, the large entrances will naturally serve as a drying room for outerwear
but will also contain a washing machine and dryer. Thus, it is reasonable to ventilate with the same
requirements as for the scullery. Table 6.3 states the ventilation requirement for the different apartments
==PAGE:137==
6.3 Ventilation principles
117
based on the Greenlandic building regulation (air change rate over area) and minimum requirement for
exhaust. For all apartments, the ventilation rate due to exhaust is greatest. However the apartments do
not require a constant air change rate this high. Therefore, a ventilation system providing a constant air
change rate adjustable for the two levels would be an appropriate solution. The ventilation requirement
and duct routing for Apartment A (4 room apt.) is considered. In Appendix C.1.1 the ventilation rates
for each room in Apartment A is given for the two air change rates. The largest air flow due to exhaust
is the dimensioning factor for choosing duct dimensions.

6.3.3
Duct routing
Fresh air is provided in the kitchen, living room and bedrooms through inlets in the ceiling. The ducts in
these rooms are hidden in duct-boxes, and are located conventionally along the walls. An air exchange
is secured by extracting stale air from the kitchen, bathroom and scullery. Constant exhaust (balanced
to supply air) in the scullery and bathroom ensures that the damp conditions does not flow to the other
rooms and small openings under the doors lets air in the rooms to avoid a vacuum effect in the rooms.
To comply with acoustic requirements, mufflers are placed on the inlet ducts straight after leaving the
air handling unit [48]. A muffler is also placed on the exhaust duct before entering the air handling
unit (AHU).
The air handling unit is placed in the entrance. There are several solutions for installing a decentral-
ized air handling unit in the apartment. Two examples are depicted below. Figure 6.11 shows a solution
for a ceiling mounted air handling unit. By lowering the ceiling 35 cm in the entrance to a room height
of approximately 2.2 m, all ducting will be hidden. The air handling unit is still accessible through
a latch in the ceiling. The submerged ceiling can also be in the hallway and bathroom for hiding the
branching of the ducts. Another possible solution is having the air handling unit placed along the wall
as seen on Figure 6.12. There are several products available dimensioned to fit in a standard 60 cm
wide closet. The ducts for the kitchen extraction and exhaustion to outdoor are hidden in a duct-box
along the wall above the door and window and led to the air handling unit. The ducting to the inlets
and bathroom are hidden above the closets in the entrance. The two exhaust ducts from the kitchen
and exhaust from other rooms are combined to a joined duct with a muffler before reaching the air
handling unit. This solution does not require a submerged ceiling in the entrance. A lowered ceiling
in the bathroom and hallway, would however be optimal for hiding the branching and ducts. Both
solutions get fresh outdoor air from an inlet above the window in the entrance, and lets out exhaust air
==PAGE:138==
118
6 Concept and design development
through the facade by the kitchen. The two suggested air handling units considered in this proposal
are from Flexit [21] and Exhausto [20].

The placements of ventilation diffusers in the rooms are not suggested as a final proposal. There
has not been made any considerations on optimal mixing or calculations on draught for this suggestion.
This proposal is merely a proposition for the duct distribution between the rooms.
The duct dimensions are a compromise between space and acoustic performance. Low air flow speed
is preferred due to noise conditions, but require larger dimensions. Appropriate duct dimensions have
been chosen with aim to correspond with the recommended flow speeds [48] and uniform sizing has
been prioritized. A diameter of 100 mm is found to be suitable for inlet and exhaust ducts to rooms,
and 250 mm for fresh air intake and exhaust to outdoors. See Appendix C.1.2 for calculations on duct
dimensions.
The two suggestions are not to be considered as the only two possible ventilation solutions for the
apartments. More as an indication of the possibilities for establishing a modern ventilation system to
ensure sufficient indoor air quality in the apartments.
==PAGE:139==
6.4 Considerations on buildability
119
6.4
Considerations on buildability
The following section describes a suggestion to how the facade could be constructed and how the
workflow of the facade replacement may unfold. A study of the original building system has given
inspiration, and the construction is based on the same principles used in the existing building. This
means a facade constructed of independent elements, composing together two and two or three and
three, as self-bearing, lightweight facade elements mounted between two slabs. The spacing in between
is handled thereafter.
Since the structural system of the apartment blocks of Paamaap Kuua, is identical or at least very
similar to many apartment blocks in Greenland, it encourage to create a simple facade system similar to
the existing. Utilizing this similarity means that the principle can be used for many apartment blocks
in Greenland without major changes.
As mentioned in Section 4.1 the facade consists of elements either 120 or 90 cm wide. The elements
can be locally prefabricated and mounted directly on the facade. Producing the elements beforehand
enables them to be constructed indoors. This allows the elements to be constructed throughout the year
independent on the climatic conditions. The prefabricated elements consists of a timber framework with
insulation (acting as the ”skeleton” of the structure), an inner layer with OSB board and an external
layer of 50 mm insulation in laths. The OSB board secures the insulation on one side, and act as a
plane surface for installing the vapour barrier at a later time. The exterior insulation must be secured,
perhaps with a temporary fixture, to stay in place during the transport and installation. All elements
are built up under the same principles but may include a window or a door.
OSB board
200 mm timber framework 
and insulation
50 mm insulation layer in 
laths
Figure 6.10: Three layers of prefabricated element - 120 cm element for reference
When the existing balconies are removed, the facade is stripped away and when the building is ready
to receive a new facade, a timber beam is to be fixed to the end of the cantilevers. The beam must be
dimensioned to maintain the new facade. Additionally, the new extended flooring may lead some forces
to the beam, but will also be carried by the existing structure. When the elements are produced, they
can be mounted on the beams. This method resembles the original fixture of the elements to the slabs.
==PAGE:140==
120
6 Concept and design development
Figure 6.11: Elements fixed to beam and internal
wall
Figure 6.12:
Windstopper from Cembrit in-
stalled
After mounting the elements, the windstopper can be installed while filling the gaps between the
elements with insulation. The 9 mm Cembrit Windstopper Extreme is a weather-resilient windstopper
appropriate for the Greenlandic weather conditions and has therefore been considered in this project.
The windstopper is fixed in assembly profiles and screwed to the element.
All joints between the
windstopper plates are thereafter sealed with the appropriate windstopper tape. Mounting the elements
and finishing the areas in between after installation was also the procedure for building the original
structure. Walls parallel to the gable must be built conventionally on-site. When all joints are sealed,
the windstopper acts as a wind and rain tight system and can function as a temporary facade (according
to manufacturer up to 12 months) [9].
When the temporary facade is established, the remaining can be coordinate accordingly. The vapour
barrier is installed from the inside on the OSB board, and the remaining internal insulation layer and
gypsum plates inserted. The ventilated exterior cladding and balconies can be erected when the climatic
condition are manageable.
==PAGE:141==
6.4 Considerations on buildability
121
6.4.1
Details of the envelope for the final renovation proposal
Details of building elements for the final renovation proposal is seen on the following page. The details
are based on the ones already presented in Section 5.1, and the following results from the energy analysis.
A) The facade system is based on earlier analysis. It consists of a core with an 200 mm insulation in
a framework. 45 mm insulation layers in laths has been added on both side of the core. The lath
dimensions has been modified slightly to match the typical available lath dimensions. In addition
the OSB plate has been added as shown to simplify the installation process of the facade element,
as explained earlier. The 9 mm CEMBRIT Windstopper is an important element in the facade
construction to ensure an air tight envelope in the rough arctic climate. The external cladding
acts as protection for rain and snow and the ventilated air cavity ensures pressure equalization.
Otherwise the external cladding defines the appearance of the building, which will be explained in
the following section. Two layers of gypsum boards on the inside ensure fire safety, and the vapor
barrier prevents moisture from inside to enter the construction and condensate. The vapor barrier
is placed such that there is space for socket outlets, without the vapor barrier is penetrated.
B) The construction of the gable has been modified slightly compared to the one explained in Section
5.1. It has been found that a solution where the existing insulation is removed would be more
pragmatic, and thus a two new insulation layers have been added to the concrete element in
the gable. The insulation layers are placed between a wooden framework that is based on the
principles of the existing system. As explained the U-value of the gable match the earlier proposed
solution. No vapor barrier is needed, since the concrete load bearing wall acts as a vapor barrier
[5], which is valid if the concrete layer is continuous with no gaps. This is realistic in this case
as all concrete in the building is cast. The aforementioned is also valid for the concrete slabs
and thus a vapor barrier is not needed here as well [37]. However, special attention needs to be
drawn to the joints between the facade system and the concrete elements. When adding the vapor
barrier to protect the facade, this has to be mounted very tightly in the joints such that moisture
are not lead inside the facade construction here.
C) In general all the thermal bridges have been eliminated, except from the ones in the first floor slab
where the load bearing inner walls joins. The effect of this thermal bridge has been decreased by
insulating the inner walls at the ground floor as explained in Section 5.2.
D) The roof is based on the existing principle of a cold attic, but the insulation thickness of the roof
slab has been increased. As explained the cold attic ensures that snow on the outside of the roof
is not heated from the interior, causing the snow to melt (this will much likely happen if the
roof is not insulated sufficiently). The melted snow can potentially freeze again leading to frost
formations, that can accumulate and cause damage to the roof and otherwise pose as a danger
for the residents.

6.5 Design choices regarding appearance

6.5
Design choices regarding appearance
Until now the external cladding and the appearance of the apartment blocks has not been discussed.
This will however be touched upon in this section.
Figure 6.13: The white blocks in Paamaap Kuua
seen from in between the buildings
Figure 6.14:
The similarity of the apartment
blocks create little change in Paamaap Kuua
As shown earlier and as seen on Figure 6.13 and Figure 6.14, the very stringent building form of the
apartment blocks in Paamaap Kuua, and the very few changes in the facades, creates a less interesting
area.
That is one of many motivations for giving the final renovation proposal a more dynamic facade.
Furthermore, the access towers and the less amount of access balconies makes it easier to point out
your home from distance. To add to the feeling of having a unique home that is different from the
others, it could be interesting to give each sets of apartments in the vertical direction a slightly different
color compared to the neighbour apartments. This will change the existing expression where every
apartment is identical, and as well give the whole area a less uniform look. The principle is seen on
a picture from a apartment block in Copenhagen (Figure 6.15) and on a sketch from a new project,
Orienten in Copenhagen (Figure 6.16).
The same principle is also evident for many buildings in Greenland, where a slight change in the
building form or a (major) change in color difference creates a sense of individual homes as seen on
Figure 6.17 (however such a high contrast difference is not necessarily needed to achieve this, as seen
on Figure 6.15 and 6.15).
==PAGE:144==

The energy analysis has not been dependent on the exterior cladding because of the ventilated
air cavity behind, and therefore the choice of external cladding has not been made. However, on the
rendered pictures of the buildings, wood has been chosen as the external cladding. No technical analysis
has been made on that material, but it is known to be easy to work with, and many of the existing
buildings in Greenland has a wooden facade. Figure 6.18 - 6.21 shows some pictures of existing larger
buildings in the arctic with a wooden facade, and a detail of a wooden facade serves as inspiration.

==PAGE:147==
CHAPTER 7
Discussion and conclusion
7.1
Discussion
In this thesis the renovation potential of apartment blocks in Greenland has been investigated, leading
to a renovation proposal of the apartment blocks of Paamaap Kuua in Sisimiut. The renovation process
is very extensive and many fields of specialization make the process very complex. To be able to realize
this project a lot of assumptions have been made, and many aspects of the renovation process are not
included. As a result, the level of detail in the renovation proposals is not very high for aspects that
do not directly impact issues regarding the thermal envelope. Instead, some aspects of the design are
described on a conceptual level, showing the principle of the project.
One of the important prerequisites of the thesis, is that the existing load bearing concrete structure
can be reused. Since the main focus of the project has been the thermal envelope, the existing struc-
tural system has not been investigated. This assumption poses as a key element for implementing the
proposed renovation strategies, and is based on valuations of competent specialists familiar with the
apartment blocks in Sisimiut. However, it is important to state that this limitation in the thesis must
be assessed more precisely before the realization of the proposal can be further developed. Addition-
ally, the evaluation was based on the Paamaap Kuua buildings in Sisimiut. The conditions and load
bearing capacities of the other apartment blocks in Greenland must be evaluated, before implementing
a renovation project.
The economic aspects have not been an area of focus in this thesis. However, a motivation for this
thesis was to evaluate if the existing building, with reuse of the structural system and infrastructure,
could be transformed into energy efficient and well-designed apartments. If this can be achieved, it could
potentially be an economically sound investment when taking demolition and new development into
consideration. However, this must be examined for evaluating the extent of potential economical benefits
of renovating. Additionally, the initiatives of reusing existing building mass are seen as a potential great
environmental benefit. These benefits may be evident when considering reuse of the energy intensive
building materials in the existing load bearing structures.
Furthermore the environmental impacts
resulting from transport of building materials to Greenland adds to this argument. However, to evaluate
the extent of environmental benefits, further analyses regarding this must be conducted.
Based on the extensive case study conducted on the apartment blocks in Sisimiut, it is considered
that the existing building mass has a great potential for undergoing a renovation. The strict system
of the buildings simplifies the process of creating a new system for managing a renovation. As the
==PAGE:148==
128
7 Discussion and conclusion
new facade system is based on the original system, it is assumed that it can be fitted in many settings
in Greenland, taken into account the extent of similarity among the apartment block in Greenland.
However, as the apartment blocks of Paamaap Kuua in Sisimiut have been the scope of reference in this
thesis, further investigation of both layout and condition needs to be carried out for similar apartment
blocks. Additionally, the presented energy performance analysis is only valid for the apartment blocks
in Sisimiut.
The thesis investigates some of the important aspects when assessing the possibilities in renovating
apartment blocks in Greenland, but further investigation has to be done, before this can be realised.
Discussion of the energy results
Many of the analyses in the thesis are based on a relatively limited amount of original drawings. In
combination with visual inspection, the overall construction of the building was assessed, but some
assumptions have been made when details have not been available. Thus it is important to understand
that the energy analysis made in this thesis might not reflect reality exactly.
Furthermore, the analyzed models are in many cases simplified to avoid too much complexity. This
is done if the simplification does not have a significant impact on the final result. However, in some
of the analyses where the thermal bridges were assessed, a minor source of error was found during the
process. When the insulation thickness in the facade system was increased to 400 mm, the criteria
for the location of cut-off planes in the geometric model were not fulfilled. The source of error was
calculated to be in the range of 1.1 % and 0.6 %, and has therefore no major impact on the results.
As the aim of the thesis has been to investigate which initiatives improve the thermal performance of
the buildings, the assumptions and simplifications do not conflict with that. Clear tendencies from the
results are still very evident, and the final results give a very good estimate of the energy consumption.
The energy performance results for the existing case are also linked with some uncertainties. The
infiltration impacts the energy consumption significantly, where it has been seen that the exhaust rate
from kitchens and bathrooms influences this. Since this value is not known, different scenarios have
been created to give an overview of which range the energy consumption is expected to be within. This
range is quite large, but all of the results show that the current energy consumption limit is not fulfilled.
Additionally, the weather file for Sisimiut does not include a wind direction, which is important when
calculating the infiltration. In the analysis the different models are rotated to see the significance of
this and again it is seen that results are not substantially influenced. Furthermore, the exact energy
consumption related to pressure loss in the ventilation system, has not been calculated. Lastly, it has to
be mentioned that the primary energy factors for Greenland have been assumed to be equal to primary
energy factors in Denmark.
The moisture analysis is linked with some uncertainties, as well. As shown, the relative humidity
depends a lot on the ventilation rates, and as exact values are not known for the existing case, the
==PAGE:149==
7.2 Conclusion
129
actual result is not exact. The main source of ventilation for the existing apartments is the infiltration.
This value is based from a blower door test conducted on one of the apartments. However a lot of
uncertainties on wind speed and exhaust rate in the apartments, as well as intentional blockage of
openings, means this value is uncertain. Additionally, the analysis is based on monthly values, which
means that peaks in temperatures and relative humidity are not accounted for.
Furthermore, the
moisture production that is compared with does not necessarily match the Greenlandic setting. Drying
wet outdoor wear indoors, might contribute to a larger moisture production. Additionally, cooking
traditions in Greenland, where food is cooked rather than fried, might add to this, as well as the fact
that life in the Greenlandic community is spent more indoors compared to Denmark. Lastly, a potential
energy recovery unit in the ventilation system will transfer more moisture to the supply air. This factor
is not accounted for in the analysis.
7.2
Conclusion
In this thesis renovation potentials of apartment blocks in Greenland produced in the 1960s and 1970s
have been investigated, which has led to a final renovation proposal of the apartment blocks of Paamaap
Kuua in Sisimiut. An optimization of the thermal envelope has been the main focus in this process.
The existing buildings have been assessed, where possibilities and constraints in the existing building
mass have been investigated. Based on the extensive case study conducted on the apartment blocks in
Sisimiut it is considered that the existing building mass constitutes as a great potential for undergoing
a renovation. The building’s strict system simplifies the process of creating a new system for managing
a renovation. Additionally, a great potential is seen in reusing the existing load bearing structure and
the existing infrastructure as well as existing pipes for water and sewage. This potential may be both
beneficial from an economical and environmental point of view, especially when the expenses related
to transport of new building materials to Greenland, are taken into account. Furthermore, the well-
designed apartments and the fact that the load bearing concrete structure has been protected by the
envelope of the buildings emphasize this. The thermal envelopes consist of non-bearing light facade
elements and is thus easily removed without interfering with the load bearing structures. The thermal
envelope is outdated, and in addition a thermography study of the buildings revealed presence of many
thermal bridges.
This has led to a thorough investigation of the heat flow in the thermal bridges, where the software
COMSOL Multiphysics has been used. The thermal bridges in the cantilevers, in the gable wings and
in the foundation have been analyzed. The results showed that the thermal bridges in the cantilevers
and the gable wings contributed substantially (13 % and 21 % respectively) compared to the overall
heat transmission through a facade element. When replacing the thermally outdated envelope with a
new one without handling the thermal bridges, the analysis shows that the thermal bridges influence
the heat loss more and more for better insulated walls. For the best insulated wall system (400 mm
insulation) it is seen that the thermal bridge in the cantilever contributes with 28 % of the total heat
==PAGE:150==
130
7 Discussion and conclusion
transmission through a facade element, whereas this is as high as 50 % for the worst case in the gable
wing. This emphasizes the need for handling the thermal bridges when renovating.
The thermal bridges in the ground floor slab were found to be very significant. Since the crawlspace
of the buildings is inaccessible, the few apartments in the ground floor have been removed and a new
thermal envelope just beneath the first floor slab has been implemented. Because of the inner load
bearing walls that join the concrete slab, a less severe but still very present thermal bridge is found in
this joint. Even though the inner walls are fully insulated, the thermal bridge in the joint between the
first floor slab and the inner walls, accounts for almost 50 % of the total heat transmission through the
first floor slab.
A important part of this thesis has been to optimize the overall energy performance of the apartment
blocks. Thus, in the second part of the energy study, the energy performance of the different scenarios
was investigated using the simulation software IDA ICE (IDA Indoor Climate and Energy). The results
for the existing case show, that the infiltration has a great impact on the energy consumption, where
the existing exhaustion rate was proven to have a major influence on the results. For the existing
case with an exhaustion rate of 60% of the current regulation, the yearly energy consumption is 243
kWh/m2 (298 kWh/m2 for 100 % exhaustion rate and 186 kWh/m2 for 20 % exhaustion rate). This
exceeds the energy consumption limit for the apartment blocks, which is 164 kWh/m2 according to the
current Greenlandic building regulation from 2006. When the existing facade is replaced with a new
one, the energy consumption drops 50 % and even more for the best insulated wall systems. The energy
consumption is further reduced when a new ventilation system with heat recovery is implemented. For
a case with a wall system with 300 mm insulation thickness the energy consumption drops from 114
kWh/m2 to 71 kWh/m2 when implementing a ventilation system. This indicates the importance of a
ventilation system with a heat recovery in the Arctic. Additionally, the analysis shows that improving
the insulation thickness from 300 mm to 400 mm only has a minor impact on the energy consumption
if the thermal bridges are not handled.
For two renovation proposal where the thermal bridges in
the facade have been eliminated (cantilever and gable wing), the energy consumption is additionally
reduced to 53.2 kWh/m2 and 58.6 kWh/m2.
Low surface temperatures at thermal bridges might lead to moisture issues, and an additional inves-
tigation on this has therefore been included in the thesis. The results show that the risk of condensation
and mould growth are very present in the existing case. This risk is decreased when implementing a
better insulated facade system, since the indoor surface temperatures increases. However, a new imple-
mented ventilation system, ensuring better ventilated apartments, decreases the risk of moisture issues
more significantly. Even though the risk of condensation and mould growth is reduced, the analysis
shows that the issue is still present if a higher moisture production than that of an average Danish
family is assumed. This might be relevant when taking into account Greenlandic conditions where
drying of wet outdoor wear, cooking methods etc. could lead to a higher moisture production. Never-
theless, the analysis emphasizes that the thermal bridges need to be handled and adds to the ambition
of eliminating the thermal bridges in the facade.
==PAGE:151==
7.2 Conclusion
131
In addition to the energy studies the functional designs of the two renovation proposals have been
investigated. The first proposal is based on the principle of the existing apartment blocks. The thermal
envelope is extended out, meaning that the cantilevers and gable wings are enclosed and thus eliminated.
Access balconies and private balconies matching the existing ones have been created with a new outdoor
structure.
The second proposal is less conventional, where the very strict facade design of the existing buildings
has been replaced by a more dynamic one. The old access system has been replaced by one with less
access balconies, creating more privacy and more daylight into the apartments. This, in combination
with the new appearance of the apartment blocks that will contribute positively to the area, makes
this proposal interesting. It shows that a renovation project like this can both improve the energy
performance and create better designed apartments, as well as improve the experience of an area.
The buildability of the new facade relies on the original system, which allows prefabricated elements
to be quickly mounted. Additionally, the system utilizes the extent of similarity in the existing building
mass. Thus, the renovation strategy is not limited to the apartment blocks in Sisimiut, but can be
applied with minor modifications in many settings in Greenland.
The aim is that these presented
solutions can serve as an inspiration for future renovations of similar apartment blocks in Greenland.

B Energy Study
B.5
Thermal bridges, calculations
In the following, the method for calculation of the linear (Ψ) and point (χ) thermal transmission is
presented. The method is described for calculation of Ψ and χ through the cantilever element in the
existing facade, including the relevant equations. Following, the results of the calculation is presented
again for the existing facade, but now including the analysis on the contribution per facade element.
This is also shown for the proposed facade system with 200 mm insulation, 400 mm insulation and 400
mm insulation with corrected cut-off planes. Lastly, the results of the heat flow analysis on the gable
wing and ground floor slab with existing building envelope is presented.

B.9 Energy performance, ventilation
157
B.9
Energy performance, ventilation
In the following the ventilation air flows is shown in detail for the existing case as well as for the three
different ventilation system scenarios in IDA ICE.
For the ventilation systems, the supply air flow rate of 0.36 l/sm2 is the required minimum rate
for the apartment as a whole, and is distributed between living rooms/kitchens [18]. The exhaustion is
distributed proportionally according to the function of the room and the corresponding exhaust air flow
requirement when in use, as described in BR2006GR. This means for instance that the bathrooms has a
higher exhaust air flow rate per m2 than the kitchen. This is however only true in the model to balance
the supply air. The actual increased exhaust air flow rate, when in use, is explained in Appendix C.1.
Since the small entrances have no exhaustion in the existing case, that also applies for the small
entrances in the proposals due to the small size and due to simplification of the model. Exhaustion has
however been implemented in the model for the new and big entrances in the proposals according to
BR2006GR.

==PAGE:187==
1
Introduction
The ambition of this report is to determine the condition of the white apartment buildings at Paamaap Quua
in the city of Sisimiut, Greenland. The buildings were constructed in the period between 1975 - 1978, and
are a representation of the Greenlandic Technical Organization (GTO) way of handling the escalating housing
requirement in the cities in Greenland in that period. Today there are six blocks standing, whilst two were torn
down in 2015. The building blocks are in general in bad shape and shows signs of many years without proper
maintenance. Furthermore some of the apartments are empty because of mould issues. After some discussion
on the existence of the rest of the building blocks at Paamaap Quua, it has recently (Spring 2018) been decided
to renovate the existing building blocks.
Figure 1: Site plan over the apartment buildings - dotted line represents demolished blocks that no longer exist
Figure 2: Block 10 (left) and Block 9 (right) on a snowy day
1
==PAGE:188==
Figure 3: The blocks of Paamaap Quua
Both the internal and external conditions have been inspected in order to determine the state of the blocks.
The inspection process was divided in two sections; external inspection and internal inspection , This report
consists ﬁrstly of a chapter explaining the methodology to this inspection. Following this, a summary of the
evaluation and ﬁndings are stated. Here lies the main conclusions drawn from the three areas of inspection.
Details on ﬁndings and the basis for the conclusion are all stated after the summary.
2
==PAGE:189==
2
Method
This chapter explains the approach for registering the condition of the apartment blocks.
Here the actual
inspection is mentioned, the grading system used for evaluating the building parts is elaborated and the use of
thermography is explained.
2.1
Inspection
An inspection of the internal conditions was conducted on the 18th. of April 2018, whilst the registration
of the external conditions was conducted on the 21st of April 2018. For the external registration, all blocks
were considered and traces of failures in the building parts were registered. For the internal registration, two
apartments were accessible to us. Both apartments were in Block 8. Therefor, Block 8 lays the basis of our
understanding of the internal conditions in the apartment blocks.
2.2
Grading system for inspection
To evaluate the condition of the building, a grading system was established for evaluating each building element.
The grading system is based on four steps. The value of the scoring is described below.
1. Good
The building part is in good shape, with minor insigniﬁcant ﬂaws.
2. Acceptable
The building part is in acceptable shape, and can be brought to good condition
with some maintenance.
3. Middle
The building part is in poor condition. There are several issues which must be
repaired in the near future to avoid any failures, violation of safety or damage
to other building elements.
4. Insuﬃcient
The building element is in a unacceptable condition, and repairs must be exe-
cuted promptly to avoid any failures, violations of safety or damages to other
building part.
3
==PAGE:190==
3
Summary
This chapter includes a summary of the ﬁndings in the inspection. For more details please read the respective
chapters following. Table 1 states the condition of the inspected building parts. The score value is described
below. Explanation of the diﬀerent scaling steps can be read in the previous chapter 2.2, Grading system for
inspection.
• 1. Good
• 2. Acceptable
• 3. Middle
• 4. Insuﬃcient

Main conclusion drawn from the external inspection
• Erosion on concrete due to ice formation, exposed reinforcement steel must be repaired
• Damages on balcony parapets must be repaired
• Doors and windows needs replacement
• Roof - not accessible for inspection but seems OK
• Stairwells are general in bad shape, showing signs of vandalism. Windows and doors to the stairwells are
destroyed or missing
• The overall expression of the building envelope - especially gables and balconies - shows signs of many
years with lack of maintenance.
Main conclusion drawn from the internal inspection
• The state of the two apartments were very diﬀerent
4
==PAGE:191==
• Both apartments had moisture damages in relation with the bathroom
• Apartment 2.06 is in need of a total renovation, the bathroom is in critical condition, the kitchen has
insuﬃcient ventilation and could use an update due to aesthetic reasons, the internal wall show traces of
moisture and all doors suﬀer damages.
• Apartment 0.13 is in better condition than 2.06 but still with some moisture issues in the shower room
(damages and traces of mould)
• In apartment 0.13 and all rooms located on the ground ﬂoor have cold surfaces near the ﬂooring of the
bearing walls
• The electrical system is old but working
5
==PAGE:192==
4
External inspection
4.1
Facade
Grade - 2
Figure 4: Elevation of facade Block 7
Figure 4 shows the facade of building Block 7. The facade is the non bearing area of the thermal envelope
and contains all doors and windows to the apartments. Some blocks have red painted cladding on the ground
ﬂoor.
The eternit plates appear faded and aging. However, most of the plates are in good shape and still intact.
There are a few examples where the plates have been damaged, see Figure 5.
Figure 5: Layers behind eternit facade plate
Figure 6: Layers of facade behind wood cladding
6
==PAGE:193==
4.2
Gable
Grade - 4
Figure 7: Elevation of gable on Block 9
The gable is the bearing element in the thermal envelope. The construction is uniform through out the
whole gable, with no window or door openings.
The overall condition of the gable is acceptable. There were some eternit plates that had been replaced by
wooden plates as seen on ﬁgure 8. As the facade, the plates on the gable also had a faded impression and there
were traces of graﬃti several places. There were several cases where the steel reinforcement in the concrete was
exposed. Most likely this is due to water penetrating cracks in the gable and expanding when freezing, which
eventually has led to blasting away of the concrete and exposed the steel to the air, causing more erosion.
Figure 8: Eternit plates exchanged with wooden plates on Block 8
7
==PAGE:194==
Figure 9: Exposed steel reinforcement
Figure 10: Exposed steel reinforcement
8
==PAGE:195==
4.3
Roof
Grade - 1
The roof was not accessible for inspection. However, it seemed to be in good condition. The cladding was a
coated metal sheet.
Figure 11: Roof on Block 7
Figure 12: Coating peeling oﬀmetal plates
Figure 13: Ventilated roof detail
9
==PAGE:196==
4.4
Foundation
Grade - 1
The foundation was seen to be in good condition. There was no subsidence or cracks to be seen on the
foundation.
Figure 14: Corner foundation between facade and
gable
Figure 15: Foundation along gable
Figure 16: Foundation
10
==PAGE:197==
4.5
Windows
Grade - 3
Figure 17: Windows
The general condition of the windows is old and have not been maintained recently. The windows have
been modiﬁed through its time in the blocks. Originally the window was a single pane window, either ﬁxed or
open-able. Later someone has attempted to improve the thermal properties of the window by ﬁxing another
single pane window to the existing frame, creating a homemade double pane window.
There were several cases were the new window frame had detached itself from the frame, loosing its thermal
beneﬁts.
Figure 18: Close up on "homemade" double pane
window
Figure 19: Inner pane open
Figure 20: Close up showing gap between the two
frames
11
==PAGE:198==
4.6
Doors
Grade - 2
The doors were worn down and had not been painted recently, besides that they seemed to be intact and in
OK condition.
Figure 21: Apartment 013 in Block 8
Figure 22: Apartment 2.06 in Block 8
12
==PAGE:199==
4.7
Balcony
Grade - 4
The blocks have balconies on each side of the facade. The frontward facing balcony is a common balcony
used to get from the stairwell to the individual apartments. This balcony is facing the direction where the
entrance to stairwell is, thus, the frontward facing facade. The balconies on the back of the block are separated
balconies accessible from each individual apartment.
The two balconies are constructed similarly. Cantilevers, ﬂooring and lower section of the parapet is all
in steel reinforced concrete and the top part of the parapet is wood. All coated in white paint. The private
balconies are separated by a screen.
There are several signs of failures on the parapet that have been ﬁxed with temporary solutions. On ﬁgure
25 - 27 some of there ﬁxtures can be seen. In general, all balconies would do good with a layer of paint.
Figure 23: Front side balcony
Figure 24: Concrete cantilevers
Figure 25: Temporary reparation of ﬁxture and
parapet
Figure 26: Replaced balcony parapet
Figure 27: Cracks and signs of abrasion on parapet
Figure 28: Balcony in need of paint
13
==PAGE:200==
4.8
Stairwell
Grade - 4
The stairwells all show traces of vandalism, graﬃti and wear. The staircase consists of a steel structure and
vinyl steps with a railing of steel and wood. The steps are slightly worn, however the staircase in itself is in
good shape. Most critical factor is that all stairwells had either doors or windows that where either broken,
missing or could not close properly.
Figure 29: Entrance to stairwell - notice the missing window
Figure 30: View of stairwell from entrance
Figure 31: View of stairwell from platform
Figure 32: Staircase ﬁxture
Figure 33: Staircase structure
14
==PAGE:201==
5
Internal inspection
Apartment 0.13 and 2.06 in Block 8 were inspected for this status report. Apartment 0.13 is a three bedroom
apartment on the ground ﬂoor. The apartment was empty due to ???. Apartment 2.06 was a single room
apartment on the second ﬂoor. The apartment was unoccupied due to mould.
5.1
Apartment 0.13
Apartment 0.13 is a three bedroom apartment with a large living room and kitchen. The toilet and shower are
in two diﬀerent rooms.
Figure 34: Plan drawing of apartment 0.13 - mirrored
15
==PAGE:202==
5.1.1
Internal walls
Grade - 2
There are both bearing and non-bearing walls in the apartment. All walls parallel with the gable are bearing,
whilst almost all walls parallel with the facade are non-bearing. The non-bearing walls are most likely built up
of wallpapered gypsum panels on a wood frame structure.
The heavy wall acts as a thermal bridge to the ground. One could feel the cold temperatures close to the
ﬂooring. There were signs of moisture on the wall between the kitchen and living room. However, this is not a
general problem for all walls.
Figure 35: Internal walls in apartment 0.13 in block 8
Figure 36:
Signs of moisture on wall between
kitchen and living room
Figure 37: Crack between adjacent walls - relevant
at have med?
16
==PAGE:203==
5.1.2
Flooring
Grade 2
There are three types of ﬂooring in the apartment. Wood ﬂooring in the living room, hallway and bedrooms,
vinyl ﬂooring in the kitchen and tiles in the entrance hallway (bathroom will be discussed later). Overall the
wood ﬂooring was in good shape, however, there were indications of moisture damages of the ﬂooring in the
living room and close to the shower room. The vinyl ﬂooring in the kitchen had also some imperfections and
bulging in some places. The tiling was in good shape, only in need of some cleaning.

Bathroom
Grade 2

Electrical installations
Grade 2
The electrical system works and seems to be in good shape. Some are older than others as seen on the
images below.
Figure 59: Examples on electrical installations in the apartment
Heating
Grade 2
There are radiators under the windows in kitchen, living room and all bedrooms. There is also a radiator
under the shoe rack in the entrance.
Figure 60: Radiator below window
Figure 61: Radiator in entrance
24
==PAGE:211==
5.2
Apartment 2.06
Apartment 206 is a single room apartment with a toilet and a small hallway separating the bedroom and kitchen.
The apartment was unoccupied due to mould.
Figure 62: Plan drawing of apartment 206
25
==PAGE:212==
5.2.1
Internal walls
Grade 3
There are both bearing and non-bearing wall in the apartment. All walls parallel with the gable are bearing,
apart from the wall between kitchen and entrance. All walls parallel with the facade are non-bearing. The
non-bearing walls are most likely built up of wood panels on a wood frame structure. The bearing concrete
walls are wallpapered.
There are several traces of moisture damages on the walls, but it is diﬃcult to determine source of moisture.
Figure 63: Layers in bearing inter-
nal wall
Figure 64: Moisture damage on wall
Figure 65: Moisture damage on wall
26
==PAGE:213==
5.2.2
Flooring
Grade 3
There is wooden ﬂooring in the bedroom, hallway and kitchen. The ﬂooring in the kitchen is worn down
and aesthetically in bad condition. The ﬂooring in the bedroom and hallway is in acceptable condition. The
entrance has tile ﬂooring which is in good condition.
Figure 66: Wood ﬂooring in bed-
room
Figure 67: Wood ﬂooring in hallway
Figure 68: Wood ﬂooring in kitchen
Figure 69: Flooring in entrance
27
==PAGE:214==
5.2.3
Ceiling
Grade 3
The ceiling in apartment 2.06 is the underside of the concrete slab which is painted. There are several signs
of moisture on the ceiling. Again, it is diﬃcult to determine the source of moisture.
Figure 70: Concrete ceiling in bedroom with traces of moisture
5.2.4
Internal doors
Grade 4
The internal doors in apartment 206 is light weight doors constructed in some sort of wood composite.
Almost all doors have been damaged and need to be replaced.
Figure 71: Door between bedroom
and hallway
Figure 72: Door to entrance hallway
with damaged frame
Figure 73: Holes found on all inter-
nal doors
28
==PAGE:215==
5.2.5
Kitchen
Grade 2
The kitchen is in OK condition, but old. There is a oven, cooker and sink. There is no cooker hood.
Figure 74: Kitchen in apartment 206
29
==PAGE:216==
5.2.6
Bathroom
Grade 4
The state of the bathroom in apartment 2.06 is very critical. The lightweight walls are painted wooden
panels ﬁxed to a wood frame. The walls are very damaged due to moisture. There are several traces of mould.
A combination of bad material choice and humidity due to insuﬃcient ventilation is most likely the explanation
of the state today. The ﬂooring and ceiling are both painted concrete. Both are very dirty and have traces of
mould. The junction between wall and ﬂooring in shower is constructed in such a way that the wooden structure
in the wall and ﬂoorboards in the bedroom are exposed.
Figure 75: Bathroom in apartment 206
30
